SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises a project to modernize and expand agro-
industries in Brazil's Center-South zone so that they can help meet rapidly
growing domestic and export market demands for processed food and feed pro-
ducts. Brazil seeks to expand and diversify exports in order to alleviate
the balance of payments constraint to economic growth. The project would
be part of the on-going export corridors program which aims at increasing
exports, especially of agricultural products, through the development of
five transportation corridors. The Grain Storage Project (857-BR, US$30
million, 1972) already contributes to this program, and a second agro-
industries project, directed toward the special problems of the Northeast,
is being prepared by the Government for possible Bank financing.
ii.       The Brazilian economy has expanded rapidly in recent years, averag-
ing 8.6% per year over the period 1966-71, although agriculture at 6.0% per
year grew more slowly than industry at 9.8% per year. Exports of the princi-
pal agricultural commodities almost doubled between 1962 and 1971, and the
expansion of non-traditional agricultural exports was particularly impres-
sive, rising from 11% of total agricultural exports in 1962 to 44% in 1971.
The food and feed processing sector has not kept pace with the growth in
agricultural output, but the proposed project would help increase the pro-
cessing capacity for some of the non-traditional agricultural exports and
reduce the import requirement for milk products.
iii.      Brazil's beef policy is aimed at achieving a target of 500,000
tons of beef exports by the end of the decade while avoiding precipitous
increases in the domestic price for beef. The latter is substantially
below the world market price at present, but the Government has expressed
its intention to permit the gradual rise of the domestic price until it
reaches the world market level. By letting the domestic price rise, by
encouraging the production of beef through various programs, and by stimu-
lating the production of substitute goods, the Government expects to offset
the effects of rising income, maintain per capita consumption of beef at
approximately the current level, and achieve the export target.
iv.       Over three years, the project would assist in financing investments
in beef processing (US$108 million), oilseed processing (US$8 million), milk
processing (US$10 million), and miscellaneous export-oriented food and feed
processing (US$9 million) for a total project cost of US$135 million. The
foreign exchange component would be about 16%. Sub-borrowers would finance
about 20% of total cost, the Government 40%, and the proposed Bank loan of
US$54 million, the remaining 40%. This would entail local expenditure financ-
ing of about US$33 million.
v.        The Bank loan would be made to the Government, which would make the
proceeds, plus its own contribution, available to the Central Bank (BC) for
channeling to six participating banks (PBs). The PBs, in turn, would onlend



- ii. -
to qualified sub-borrowers for periods of up to 10 years, including a maximum
of two years' grace, at an interest rate of 5%, plus full indexing.
vi.       Sub-loans would be made primarily to existing enterprises for ex-
pansion or for rehabilitation of facilities to enable them to adopt new tech-
nology and/or to meet new sanitary standards (beef processing). Total capacity
of project enterprises would be about 22,000 head/day of cattle, 1,200 tons/day
of oilseeds (principally soybeans), 500,000 1/day of milk, and several hundred
tons per day of other commodities such as fruits and grains.
vii.      BC would be responsible for overall execution of the project and
would establish a Project Team to administer day-to-day operations and review
the work of the PBs.  The PBs would appraise sub-projects according to criteria
set forth in subsidiary loan agreements to be signed with BC and would submit
their appraisals to BC for review. BC would set sub-loan approval limits for
the PBs as it deemed appropriate. After sub-loans were made, PBs would super-
vise progress of each sub-borrower in implementing his plan. The PBs are
financially sound, have competent management and staff, and are fully capable
of undertaking the appraisal and supervision of sub-projects.
viii.     Procurement would be undertaken according to the requirements of
the particular enterprise involved, as decided by the enterprise and the
participating bank. At least three quotations would be obtained by sub-bor-
rowers from contractors or manufacturers and submitted to the PBs for review.
Brazilian contractors and manufacturers of the types of facilities to be uti-
lized in the project have costs comparable to international firms and have
demonstrated an ability to compete for international contracts. Most major
foreign firms are represented in Brazil through affiliates or sales agents.
ix.       Financial rates of return, ranging from 13% to 24%, would provide
incentives adequate to induce investors to participate in the project. The
economic rate of return of the project would be about 53% for beef process-
ing, 23% for oilseed processing, and 48% for milk processing. The project
would also contribute to employment through the creation of jobs during the
implementation period as well as in the operational phase.
x.        With appropriate assurances, the project is suitable for a Bank
loan of US$54 million for a term of 15 years, including a grace period of
five years. The Government of Brazil would be the borrower and would assume
the foreign exchange risk.

0                                 AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of Brazil has requested an IBRD loan of US$54 million
to assist the development of agro-industries in the 10 central and southern
states of the country. The proposed project would be part of Brazil's on-going
export corridors program which seeks to increase exports, especially of agri-
cultural products, through the development of five transportation corridors,
four in the central and southern states and one in the Northeast. The Grain
Storage Project (857-BR, US$30 million, 1972) also contributes to the program.
In addition to 857-BR, Bank lending for Brazil's agricultural sector consists
of two loans for livestock development (516-BR, US$40 million, 1967; and 868-BR,
US$26 million, 1972), and a loan for agricultural settlement in the nottheast
area (853-BR, US$6.7 million, 1972). A second agro-industries project, which
will be designed to provide an expanded market for the agricultural products
of the Northeast, is being prepared by the Government. Also being prepared
is a third livestock development project, designed to ensure the continuity
of the program to modernize and expand livestock production in the Center-
South, and to extend that program to smaller farmers.
1.02      The proposed agro-industries project was prepared by the Central
Bank (BC) in 1972 with the assistance of consultants and on the basis of re-
commendations made by a Bank team which visited Brazil early in 1972.  This
appraisal report is based on the findings of an appraisal team, composed of
Messrs. T. Davis, C. Cronberg, G. Luhman, D. Steel, and J. Zarandin (Bank)
and D. Evans, R. Somers and A. Weinstock (consultants), which visited Brazil
in November/December 1972.
II. GENERAL ECONOMY AND AGRICULTURAL SECTOR
2.01      The Brazilian economy has grown at a remarkable rate in recent years.
In the 1966-71 period, the GDP showed an annual growth rate of 8.6%; industry,
9.8%; and agriculture, 6.0% (Annex 1). Average per capita income increased
at about 5.4% in this period, and passed the US$400 level in 1971, in spite
of a population growth rate of about 3% a year (population reached 94.5 millioni
in 1970 and was around 100 million in 1972). Some 56% of the people live in
cities and towns, and migration from rural areas has reduced the rate of growth
of the rural population to less than 1% a year.
2.02      Although its absolute contribution to GDP continues to increase
rapidly, agriculture now accounts for only one-fifth of GDP, as compared to
one-third in the late 1940s. Bringing new land into production accounts for
much of the growth in agriculture. Very substantial increases in acreage of
major crops took place in the 1965-71 period: beans, manioc, corn, and peanuts,
between 14 and 24%; oranges, 44%; wheat, nearly 200%; and soybeans, almost 270%.



- 2 -
In contrast, coffee, long the major foreign exchange earner for Brazil, fell
in acreage to 2.59 million ha in 1971, a 26% decline from 1965. However,
yields increased sharply in this period, and production is back at the same
level as in 1965.
2.03      The dynamism of the agricultural sector has not been limited to
production.  Exports of the principal agricultural commodities almost doubled
in the 1962-71 period, and reached over US$1.74 billion in 1971, equivalent
to about 61% of all merchandise exports. The performance of non-traditional
agricultural exports has been particularly impressive:
Exports
1965           1971         Increase 1965-71
----US$ million----               %---%…
Beef (Fresh)              24              99                300
Beef (Processed)          12              51                325
Soybean Meal               7              78              1,000
Corn                      28              75                170
Orange Juice               2              36              1,700
Soybeans                   7              24               240
This rapid expansion of non-traditional agricultural exports was sufficient
to increase their contribution to total agricultural exports from 11% in 1962
to 44% in 1971, in rDite of a continuing growth (3.6% per year) in traditional
agricultural exports (coffee, cotton, sugar, cocoa, and tobacco).
2.04      Although milk production has not grown as rapidly as production
of other agricultural commodities, it rose about 38% from 1965 to 1970.  Im-
ports of milk products have varied considerably from year to year but reached
a level of 23,000 tons of milk equivalent in 1970 and are projected by FAO
to increase substantially by the end of the decade.
2.05      Growth of the food and feed processing sector has not kept pace
with the growth in output and exports of non-traditional products (in terms
of value added, the sector expanded at a rate of 3.6% in the 1966-71 period).
The proposed project would help to increase processing capacity for some of
the key items on the non-traditional export list for which both export and
domestic demand is expected to grow rapidly--especially beef and soybean meal--
and would help to reduce the import requirement for milk.
2.06      As a complement to this project, the Government has engaged consul-
tants to prepare a development project for agro-industries in the Northeast
which is expected to be completed by mid-1973. In spite of heavy migration
from the Northeast, and a substantial transfer of resources from Brazil's
Center-South region to the Northeast in recent years, the area continues to
lag behind the rest of Brazil in its rate of economic growth. Severe social
problems persist there, and average per capita income of its 29 million people
is estimated to be about one-half of the national average. The Northeast
project now being prepared will involve both on-farm development and product



-3-
processing, as the present agricultural production base in the Northeast is
not sufficient to support processing industries of significant scale.
III. PROJECr BACKGROUND
A. Proiect Area
3.01      The project would be located in the 10 states of Brazil's relatively
well developed Center-South T/'where about 78% of Brazil's beef, all of its
soya, about 80% of its milk, and 85% of its fruit for juice, is produced.
(Maps 10335, 10336, and 10386). Beef production occurs throughout the region,
with the greatest concentration in Rio Grande do Sul, the southernmost state
of Brazil. Soya production is concentrated in the southern half of the region,
while milk production is centered in its northern part and fruit juice pro-
duction is mainly in Sao Paulo. Climate in the area ii-mo4erate and rainfall
is relatively high, averaging -betwe-nft-1^2-00--and 2,000, mm pei year, with a rainy
seasoin in the suoir (December to April). Soils, farm sizes and types, and
vast land resources are suitable for production purposes. The processing
industries are expanding and undergoing rapid modernization, particularly in
the beef and soya processing sectors.
3.02      Compared with the other regions of Brazil, the Center-South has a
relatively well developed transport system. Major highways have been rapidly
expanded and improved in recent years and work is being continued under a
mater plan, partly assisted by the Bank. The rural road system is also re-
ceiving increased attention. A program to develop 10,000 km of rural roads
has begun recently and will be especially beneficial to the state of Rio
Grande do Sul. The railroad system is being upgraded with new line construc-
tion, rolling stock improvements, and line integration. Major port improve-
ment programs are now underway in Victoria (ES), Santos (SP), and Rio Grande
(RS). The objectives of the latter are to improve road and rail access,
deepen harbors, and greatly enlarge cold and dry storage facilities and load-
ing equipment.
B. Production of Project Commodities
Beef Production
3.03      Brazil's cattle herd, estimated at 84.8 million head in 1971, is
the world's third largest.  Almost 50% are identified as beef cattle, with
another 35% as mixed beef-dairy and the remaining 15% as mainly dairy.
Average annual growth rates of the herd, based on census data, were 1.8%
for 1950-60 and 3.9% for 1960-70.
1/   Rio Grande do Sul (RS); Guanabara (GB); Santa Catarina (SC); Parana (PR);
Sao Paulo (SP); Rio de Janeiro (RJ); Espirito Santo (ES); Minas Gerais
(MG); Goias (GO), including the Federal District; and Mato Grosso (MT).



-4
3.04      The average annual growth rate of beef production between 1952-54
and 1967-69 was about 3.6%, while average yield, expressed in carcass weight
per head, increased during this period from 161 kg to 193 kg. While world
imports of beef rose from about 1.94 million tons in 1965/66 to 2.61 million
tons in 1970/71, an annual increase of about 6%, Brazil's exports jumped
from 30,000 tons in 1966 to 174,000 tons in 1972, an increase of about 34%
per year. Annex 2 presents data on beef production, including pertinent
classifications and detailed discussion on Brazil's livestock and meat pro-
cessing sector.
Oilseed Production
3.05      The principal project-related oilseeds are soybean, cottonseed,
and peanut, and, to a limited extent, castor seed. Their processing results
in basically two important groups of products, namely, vegetable oil and
oilcakes and meals. Soybean is the most important of these oilseeds (Annex 3)
and will probably increase in importance in future years. Between 1958-60
and 1969-71, annual soybean output grew at a rate of about 22% per year, while
the annual outputs of cottonseed, castor beans, and peanuts increased at rates
varying from 4 to 8% per year. The latest estimate of the 1971/72 soybean
crop indicates a record harvest of about 3.4 million tons and it is expected
to be exceeded by a 1972/73 harvest of 4.2 million tons, mainly as a result
of rapid expansion of area planted. In the case of soybean oil and cake/
meal, the annual growth rate during the period 1958-60 to 1969-71 was about
14%, and those for cottonseed, castor bean, and peanut oil and cake/meal
ranged from 5 to 8% (Annex 3).
Milk Production
3.06      The main products of the milk processing industry in Brazil are
pasteurized fresh milk, butter, cheese, powdered milk, and condensed milk.
While the production of raw milk increased at an annual rate of about 4%
between 1958-60 and 1968-70, output of pasteurized milk and powdered milk
grew more rapidly, at about 11% and 9% per year, respectively. 1/ Output
of cheese and condensed milk grew more slowly during the period, while butter
production declined somewhat (Annex 4).
Miscellaneous Food and Feed Production
3.07      A number of commodities offer good prospects for export in processed
form, among them citrus fruit and manioc (Annex 5). Annual output of citrus
fruit and manioc increased at a rate of about 6% per year between 1958-60 and
1968-70.
1/   The growth rate for pasteurized milk is for the period 1958-60 to 1969-71.
The figures anid trc..ds for processed products are based on information
from the Inspection Division for Products of Animal Origin.

C.  Government Policies Pertaining to Project Industries
Beef
3.08      Brazil's aims to export 500,000 tons of beef annually by 1980 with-
out causing precipitous increases in its domestic price, which was only about
60% of the corresponding FOB export price in 1972 (Annex 2). To help attain
this export target, the Government intends to strengthen the production pro-
gram for beef through such measures as aftosa 1/ control, improve pastures,
and better breed selqction, while allowing only gradual increases in
the real domestic price of beef.  In addition, it plans to encourage
pork and poultry production to meet much of the incremental demand for
meats resulting from rising per-capita income. However, raising beef,
pork and poultry output takes time and measures promoting such increases
cannot have a substantial impact in the immediate future. As an interim
policy, therefore, the Government has adopted a series of temporary measures
to hold beef exports to levels consistent with gradual increases in the
domestic beef price.
3.09      Among the measures adopted are export quotas, an export tax, maximum
domestic prices to processors and to farmers, and a beef storage requirement
tied to exported quantities (designed to even out seasonal fluctuations in
supply for the domestic market). The maximum prices are set by informal Gov-
ernment agreements with farmers, processors and the retail trade, and enforc-
ed by an inspection system, care being taken to allow adequate returns to
farmers and processors (para. 4.04 and Annex 2).  Eventually, as the domestic
price of beef gradually rises to a level consistent with its FOB export price,
there would be no more need for these measures.
3.10      The Government attaches great importance to the domestic price of
beef because beef is an important item in the general price index. A primary
national goal is to hold domestic price inflation in 1973 to not more than 12%,
which would be the lowest in well over a decade. As general inflationary forces
are dampened, however, gradual real increases in the price of beef will become
less important. As a result, the domestic price of beef can rise to a level
consistent with its FOB export price and, with vigorous implementation of the
meat production program, enable Brazil to reach its beef export target of
500,000 tons annually by 1980 without any perceptible impact on general price
level movements.
3.11      Another element in the Government's policy is its drive to raise the
hygiene standards of meat processing plants (Annex 2). New federal sanitary
regulations were established at the end of 1971 and are being applied to all
plants on a state-by-state basis, starting in the South, by the Inspection
Division for Products of Animal Origin (DIPOA) of the Ministry of Agriculture.
Plants which cannot comply with the standards are closed and those which re-
quire modifications are given stipulated periods of time within which to com-
ply. All plans for construction or modification must be approved by DIPOA,
and DIPOA also issues export licenses when plants meet the hygiene requirements
of importing countries (para. 3.17).
1/ Foot-and-mouth disease.



-6-
Oilseeds
3.12      The price support program for soybeans, the primary oilseed to be
processed under the project, is part of the general agricultural price support
program which includes all grains except wheat, for which there is a separate
program (Annex 3). The program consists of two elements: purchase of com-
modities at the minimum price and short-term financing. No soybeans have been
purchased, since the market price has remained high, but producers have made
substantial use of the short-term financing facility, which has stimulated
the production of soybeans.
3.13      In response to a slump in sales of vegetable oil in 1966-68, the
Government discouraged the expansion of oilseed processing capacity (Annex 3).
In recent years, however, production of soybean products, in particular, has
grown rapidly as a result of the strong increase in the demand for meal. As
a result, the Government has relaxed its restrictions (mainly on the provision
of long-term financing), and capacity has expanded substantially over the past
two years.
Milk
3.14      The Government intends to reduce imports of milk by stimulating
production through the establishment of milk basins (Map 10386) and the
provision of additional technical and financial assistance (Annex 4). The
price-support prograr, which sets minimum prices for Type C milk to producers,
seeks to encourage production and reduce seasonal fluctuations in supply, while
the controlled wholesale price to processors of Type C milk is intended to
keep the consumer price low. Since prices for other types of milk and milk
products are not controlled, however, average margins to processors are ade-
quate. Although all milk processing plants are subject to inspection by
DIPOA, the agency only inspects perhaps half of the plants now operating.
Plans for construction or modification of plants are to be submitted to
DIPOA for approval.
Miscellaneous
3.15      All grains except wheat (for which there is a separate program)
are included in the general agricultural price support program (para 3.12).
There are no similar programs for citrus fruits.
D. Project Food and Feed Processing Industries
lieef Processing
1.16      Prior to implementation of the new sanitary regulations, the beef
processing industry consisted of perhaps 2,000 units, of which over 80% had a
capacity of less than 50 head/day, somewhat more than 10% had 50-200 head/day,



-9-
demand for processed products based on these commodities. Furthermore, agro-
industry in Brazil is following the trend in the U.S. and Europe toward more
specialized firms. The level of tethnology in Brazil generally is on a par
with that in other major producing countries, and the country has a large
supply of labor with relatively low wages. Products such as fruit juice and
feed meal have good prospects.  Brazil now has about one-third of the total
world market for concentrated orange juice, and its share continues to in-
crease. Demand for meal is also growing, the product being used as supple-
mental feed for livestock, especially poultry and pigs. The investment needs
are substantial and increasing as a result of the growth and diversification
of the industries. (See Annex 5.)
E. Sources and Availability of Funds
3.27      As is the case for most other small- and medium-sized industries in
Brazil, agro-industries must rely heavily on internally generated funds to
meet their needs. Short-term financing of working capital is available from
commercial banks at a real interest rate of about 9% (the Bank of Brazil
charges a nominal rate of around 19%, or a real rate of about 3-1/2% 1/).
For export firms, there is also short-term financing of export production
from Government funds available through commercial banks at a real interest
rate of about -6%. Cooperatives have access to some credit (largely short
term) from the National Cooperative Credit Bank (BNCC). Remaining short-term
needs are met by the firms themselves.
3.28      The real pinch is in long-term credit.  There is some medium- and
long-term financing of construction and equipment available (primarily through
the state development banks) from funds administered principally by BC and
the National Bank for Economic Development (BNDE) at real interest rates rang-
ing generally from 4 to 8%. BNCC also makes a small amount of longer term
credit available to cooperatives. These funds are extremely limited, however,
and most firms must supply virtually all of their long-term funds from internal
sources or from the rollover of short-term financing. In many cases, invest-
ments have been postponed or simply not undertaken because of the lack of
funds. A discussion of the existing institutional and credit framework is
given in Annex 6.
IV. THE PROJECT
A. General Description
Definition
4.01      The project would be part of the lending programs of five state
development banks and the Bank of Brazil in the 10 central and southern states
1/   Taking the rate of inflation at 15%, the rate in 1972.



- 10 -
of Brazil to further the growth of exports of processed agricultural products
and reduce imports of milk products. Over a period of three years, it would
help finance rehabilitation and new construction of processing facilities for
beef (80% of total investments), oilseed (6%), milk (7%), and miscellaneous
xport-oriented food and feed products (7%). It would also include elements
for training of technicians in the sub-projects.
Lending Program
4.02      Total estimated cost of the project is Cr$818 million (US$135 mil-
lion), of which Cr$654 million (US$108 million) would be financed by loans
from the participating banks (PBs) and Cr$164 million (US$27 million) would
be provided by sub-borrowers. The Bank's contribution would be US$54 million.
The number and size of sub-loans under each component would vary considerably
according to the types of investments involved. The cost phasing is shown
in Annex 7 and commitment of sub-loans is indicated below;

B. Detailed Features
Beef Processing
4.03      Substantial investments in beef processing facilities are required
to enable processors to meet new federal sanitary regulations and hygiene re-
quirements of importing countries (para. 3.11) and to supply more fully pro-
cessed products to the international market. Investments under the project
would be made in some 75 plants located throughout the 10 central and southern
states, although higher concentrations would be found in Rio Grande do Sul,
Sao Paulo, and Minas Gerais. Most work would involve the rehabilitation of
existing facilities, but a few new plants would also be constructed. Reha-
bilitation would range from an expansion of cold storage capacity to more
extensive improvements involving combinations of processing, refrigeration,
and cold storage facilities. Cold storage facilities intended for beef would
be financed even if they were built and operated by firms which are not pro-
cessors. Capacities of project plants would range from about 200 to 800
head/day. Investments would also be made in about 20 refrigerated trucks,
which would be purchased by meat processors or independent firms that provid-
ed refrigerated transport services to the beef processing industry. The form



-~~~~~~~-1
which this component takes and its success, depend upon the behavior of in-
dividuals and groups acting independently. They are considered to have suf-
ficient managerial capability, command adequate technical know-how, and possess
sufficient knowledge of the industry and its supply and demand characteristics
to enable them to make and implement appropriate decisions.  This judgment
also applies to the other components of the project.
4.04      During negotiations, the Government assured the Bank that it would
let the domestic beef prices rise gradually to world market level and that,
in any case, it intends to maintain the domestic price at a level which would
provide adequate returns to producers and processors (para. 3.09). Since the
aftosa control program (para. 3.08) is important to the adequate supply of
cattle for project plants, the program should be initiated in the States of
Goias and Mato Grosso during 1974 and expanded to include a larger percentage
of the herd in project areas where it already has been started. It is also
essential that the plan for implementation of the hygiene regulations be car-
ried cut. In order to enable DIPOA to do this, the Government will strengthen
the DIPOA staff by retaining competent and experienced personnel in sufficient
numbers. Assurances on the above were obtained during negotiations. That the
beef export regulations, which are currently out of date, had been updated
would be a condition of effectiveness of the proposed loan and assurances
were obtained that the regulations would continue to be updated thereafter.
Oilseed Processing
4.05      The major part of the loan would be for modernizing old crushing
units, converting them into efficient working units of medium size with solvent
extraction. Some cottonseed and peanuts would be processed but soybeans would
be the primary raw material. Capacity of remodelled facilities would range
from about 250 tons/day to about 700 tons/day, so that advanced processing
techniques, utilizing solvents, could be used and efficient levels of produc-
tion achieved. Use of solvents would permit an increase in the amount of oil
extracted from 13% to 16.5%. Approximately four sub-projects are expected
to be financed in the states of Rio Grande do Sul, Parana, and Minas Gerais
(Map 10386). Except in special cases where factors such as location war-
ranted, no refining facilities would be financed, because existing capacity
is probably adequate for the next few years (para. 3.22).
Milk Processing
4.06      About 10 milk processing sub-projects are expected to be financed
under the project, the majority to be situated in the states of Minas Gerais
and Sao Paulo (para. 3.24 and Map 10386). Plant capacity would range from
about 30,000 liters/day (raw material) to about 100,000 liters/day. Since
the demand for milk products is growing rapidly, many of the sub-projects
would involve completely new plants, although several existing plants would
be rehabilitated. A special feature of the sub-projects would be the emphasis
on more highly processed products such as powdered milk and cheese. In the



-12-
analysis of sub-projects, particular attention would be given to the adequacy
of the milk supply for existing and proposed facilities. Partly as a result
of the current emphasis on the beef industry, inspection of milk plants is not
as widespread or thorough as would be desirable, and an assurance was obtained
at negotiations that the sanitary regulations regarding milk processing would
be strictly applied to plants included in the project.
Miscellaneous Food and Feed Processing
4.07      Sub-projects under this category would cover a range of export-
oriented food and feed processing facilities, an example being the production
of frozen fruit juice concentrates. In most cases, the establishment of a
new plant would be required, the majority to be located in the states of
Sao Paulo, Minas Gerais, and Espirito Santo. About seven sub-projects would
be financed.

exchange requirements. Estimated project costs are given below and the phasing
is indicated in Annex 7.

4.09      The estimates are based on November 1972 domestic prices and are

of 10% and a price contingency of 10% are included, the latter to cover possi-
ble real price increases in dollar terms of about 5% per year over the three-
year project period. Project costs are based on those for similar facilities
recently financed by the PBs and data received from Brazilian manufacturers
and consulting firms.

0            4.11      The Bank loan of US$54 million would finance about 40% of total
project cost, including the estimated foreign exchange cost of about US$21
million and about US$33 million of local cost. The Government would contri-
bute 40% and the balance would be provided by sub-borrower from their own
resources, or additional debt financing from non project funds. PBs would,
however, require sub-borrowers to have prudent debt/equity ratios (Schedule
A).
4.12      The Bank loan, to be disbursed over five years, would be made to
Government which would bear the foreign exchange risk. Government, through
BC, would onlend to the PBs under subsidiary loan agreements satisfactory to
the Bank specifying the types of sub-projects eligible for financing, the
percentage to be financed, the rate of interest, maturity dates, methods of
repayment and procurement, and the auditing of accounts. The signing of at
least one subsidiary loan agreement, incorporating the provisions of Schedule
A, would be a condition of effectiveness.
1/   When the US dollar was recently devalued, the cruzeiro was also adjusted.
The net result was a revaluation of the cruzeiro relative to the dollar
by about 3%. Since the foreign exchange component of the project is
approximately 16%, the change in total project cost as a result of the
currency adjustment would be less than 1%. This amount is lower than
any reasonable margin of error in the cost estimates and therefore the
estitates were left unchanged.



- 14 -
4.13      Loans to sub-borrowers would have terms of up to 10 years, and,
with loans commencing in three successive years, the last repayments would
be made 13 years after the start of the project. The cash flow projection
for BC indicates that sub-borrowers' debt service payments (at an interest
rate of 5% with full indexing) would generate a sufficient inflow to repay
the Bank loan within 15 years, including five years' grace and to cover a
reasonable degree of slippage in payments (Annex 8).
E. Procurement
4.14      Procurement procedures would vary from case to case and would be
decided upon by the sub-borrowers, with the concurrence of the PBs, thus
following the pattern of Bank loans to development finance companies. The
PBs presently review the procurement decisions of their clients, but, in
order to promote uniformity of approach, PBs would require that sub-borrowers
under the project obtain quotations from at least three contractors or sup-
pliers and submit them to the PBs for review (Schedule A).
4.15      Brazil produces a wide range of equipment to be utilized in the
project and its firms have sufficient capacity to supply the goods and ser-
vices required. Although import duties on this equipment vary from about
30% to 50% (there arc no quantitative restrictions), competitive conditions
in the country are such that production costs of domestic equipment are com-
parable to the CIF cost of equipment provided by foreign suppliers.  Brazilian          0
contractors and manufacturers have demonstrated an ability to compete for
the types of facilities to be utilized in the project at costs competitive
with international firms. If a particular item is not produced in Brazil,
the law permits an exemption from import duties. Foreign firms are widely
represented by domestic affiliates, which would be expected to bid for
project contracts.
F. Disbursement
4.16      Disbursement of the Bank loan would be made against 50% of BC re-
financing of sub-loans disbursed by PBs to sub-borrowers. A condition of
disbursement, in the case of each PB, would be the signing of a subsidiary
loan agreement between BC and that PB. PBs generally disburse directly to
suppliers, except in special circumstances, but this procedure would be
made a requirement under the project to ensure uniformity (Schedule A). All
?Bs make disbursements in installments and ensure that implementation of
eachi stage has been completed and that acquisitions correspond to disburse-
ments before releasing the next installment.
4.i7      Disbursement requests to the Bank would be accompanied by state-
ments of expenditures certified by BC. The supporting documentation would
.



- 15 -
be retained by the PBs for review by the Bank during supervision.  The PBs'
disbursement to sub-borrowers would take about 4-1/2 years and disbursement
of the Bank loan is expected to take five years. The estimated schedule of
disbursements is given in Annex 9.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Project Administration
5.01      BC would administer the project and, in order to perform its func-
tions, would establish a Project Team (PT) which would review appraisals of
sub-projects submitted to it by the PBs, supervise the operations of the PBs
relating to the project, and carry out the other duties of BC under the proj-
ect. PT would be headed by a Project Coordinator and would include in its
initial staff (a) a financial analyst, (b) an economist, (c) a veterinarian,
and (d) a processing engineer with extensive experience in beef processing.
The qualifications and the terms of reference for these personnel, including
any replacements, would be subject to consultation between BC and the Bank.
That the PT had been so established and staffed would be a condition of loan
effectiveness. To supplement PT staff, consultants would be retained per- V
iodically as needed to review the technical viability of sub-projects. As-
surances on the above were obtained during negotiations.
5.02      PT would conduct a continuing review of the industries included in
the project in order to ascertain whether capacity increases to be financed
were compatible with overall supply and demand, taking into account expansion
of facilities outside the project.  The review would complement those on a
state and regional basis to be undertaken by the PBs.  PT would also keep
abreast of technological advances of project industries to enable it to judge
the technical viability of proposed sub-projects and would collect data to
determine the financial and economic benefits resulting from the project and
to form the basis for further investment in agro-industries. As part of its
supervisory function, PT staff would make periodic visits to PBs to review
their operations under the project. Assurances on these points were obtained
during negotiations.
B. Participating Banks
5.03      The Bank of Brazil (BB); the state development banks of Minas Gerais
(BDMG), Espirito Santo (BANDES), Sao Paulo (BADESP), and Parana (BADEP); and



- 16 -
the Regional Development Bank for the Extreme South (BRDE) 1/ would participate
in the project (Annex 6).
5.04      Each state development bank would lend to sub-borrowers within its
respective sphere of operations, while BB would lend throughout the project
area and carry sole responsibility in those states not covered by the other
participating banks: Mato Grosso; Goias, including The Federal District;
Rio de Janeiro; and Quanabara. PBs would be individually responsible for
appraisal, supervision and collection of sub-loans, and would assume the
financial risk.
State Development Banks
5.05      General.  The state development banks operate within the framework
of federal and, particularly, state policies, and seek to promote economic
development by providing long-term credit to enterprises in priority sectors.
Although they are small in comparison to BB-7-the assetsof those partici- -
pating in the project at the end of 1971 ranged from Cr$85 million 2f to-Cr$454
million, or US$15 million to US$81 million), they have been an important source
of long-term financing for industry (Annex 6), and especially in agro-industry.
The state development banks that would participate in the project are compara-
tively new institutions, all of them having been established within the last
decade, some as successors to state development corporations. BDMG, BANDES,
BADESP, and BADEP are wholly or nearly wholly owned enterprises of the respec-
tive governments of the states of Minas Gerais, Espirito Santo, Sao Paulo,
and Parana, while BRDE is owned by the governments of Rio Grande do Sul,
Santa Catarina, and Parana. Each is governed by specific laws enacted by the
state legislatures but all are subject to the overall control of the BC under
the terms of Monetary Council Resolution No. 93 of 1968 as amended. State
development banks are not permitted to operate branch offices, but this re-
striction is not a constraint on their activities. Management is satisfactory
and the banks would constitute a satisfactory channel for project funds.
5.06      Source and Use of Resources.  In addition to their own capital and
reserves and, in the case of a few banks, deposits from the public, partici-
pating state development banks have access to various federal and state special
funds (Annex 6). The principal sources of federal financing are BC and the
National Development Bank (BNDE), although the Fund for Studies and Project
Planning (FINEP), controlled by the Federal Ministry of Planning, is being
increasingly used to finance project feasibility studies. The General Fund
for Agriculture and Industry (FUNAGRI) administered by BC and the Fund for
the Development of Small and Medium Industry (FIPEME) and the Special Indus-
trial Financing Agency (FINAME) administered by BNDE are of particular
importance.
1/   The Regional Development Bank for the Extreme South (BRDE) operates in
the states of Rio Grande so Sul, Santa Catarina, and Parana. However,
the regulations governing the state and regional development banks are
essentially the same, and the term "state development bank" is used to
include BRDE as well.
2/   All figures relating to operations of PBs are in terms of 1971 cruzeiros           0
(and 1971 dollars), unless otherwise specified.



- 17 -
5.07      Loans outstanding increased from Cr$594 million (US$106 million) at
the end of 1969 to Cr$1101 million (US$197 million) at the end of 1971. The
major portion was for industry. Lending rates vary substantially, depending
on loan purposes and source of funds. Most medium- and long-term loans are
adjusted according to the movements of either the National Treasury Bond
Index (ORTN), which is linked to the general price index, or of the cruzeiro/
dollar exchange rate.  Loans from FINEP funds carry an interest rate of 16%
per annum to sub-borrowers. FIPEME-financed loans have an interest rate of
4% per annum on outstanding balances adjusted for the change in the ORTN index,
while long-term loans from FINAME funds bear interest at 7% per annum on out-
standing balances subject to ORTN indexing. The banks' margins on loans
financed with borrowed funds are between 2% and 3%. Interest rates on loans
from the banks' own resources are generally in line with those charged on
loans financed with borrowed funds.
5.08      Financial Position.  The financial position of the participating
state development banks is quite sound (see balance sheets and income state-
ments appended to Annex 6). Loans have been made on the basis of competent
appraisals, and arrears to date have been reasonable at about 1% of portfolio.
In some cases, profits have been insufficient to compensate for the effects
of inflation on capital and reserves but this situation is expected to improve
as the banks reach more efficient levels of operation and obtain more adequate
margins on lending.
Bank of Brazil
5.09      BB is the country's largest commercial bank (with assets of Cr$47.8
billion, or US$8.5 billion, at the end of 1971) and is expanding its operations
in agro-industry as a complement to its primary emphasis on agriculture. It
performed the functions of a central bank until 1964 and still acts as finan-
cial agent for the Federal Treasury and other government agencies, but its
main objective is to promote and advance national production. It is execu-
ting agency for the Grain Storage Project (857-BR, US$30 million, 1972).
Government holds 55% of BB's share capital (Cr$1.8 billion, or about US$321
million, in 1972) and the rest is held by the private sector. The President
of the Republic nominates BB's president and one director, while 11 directors
are elected by shareholders. At end September 1972, BB had 810 branches
distributed throughout Brazil, of which 599 were in the 10 states comprising
the project area. BB would provide an effective complement to the state de-
velopment banks and would be an appropriate channel for all project funds in
those areas not covered by the other banks.
5.10      BB's main source of funds is deposits (short term, sight, and demand)
from the public, banks, other financial institutions, the Treasury, and BC.
BB's loans outstanding to the private sector at end 1971 totalled Cr$22.5
billion (US$4.1 billion), of which Cr$8.7 billion were for industry, including
Cr$1.7 billion for food production. Interest rates, monetary correction rates,
other charges, loan and grace periods, and security requirements also vary ac-
cording to the size and the purpose of the loan and the source of funds. In
1971, the weighted annual average yield (interest and other charges) on all
loans was 14.7%.



- 18 -
C.  Project Fund and Project Account
5.11      The Central Bank would establish a separate Project Fund, into
which would be paid the proceeds of the Bank loan, plus Government's contribu-
tion. PBs would draw from this fund to cover sub-loan disbursements as stipu-
lated in the subsidiary loan agreements (Schedule A). BC would establish a
Project Account which would reflect the operations and financial conditions
of the Project Fund and of BC with respect to all sub-lending transactions
within the framework of the project. In addition, each PB would maintain an
azcount which would reflect the status of the financing received by it under
the project.
5.12      PBs would draw from the Project Fund 100% of approved loans disbursed.
They would pay the Project Fund a rate of 2-1/2%, with indexing based on ORTN
prices, and would lend to sub-borrowers at 5%, also with indexing based on ORTN
prices.
D. Lending Policies and Procedures
Sub-loan Appraisal and Supervision
5.13      Potential sub-borrowers would prepare detailed feasibility studies
covering the technical, organizational, financial, and commercial aspects of
their sub-projects, which would determine the soundness of the investments
and the creditworthiness of the applicants (Schedule A). Each sub-project
appraisal would be submitted to BC for review. Processing facilities would
have to be approved by DIPOA, as required by law (paras. 3.11 and 3.14). BC
would set sub-loan approval limits for PBs as it judged appropriate. Since
some of the appraisal procedures recommended under the project are relatively
new to the PBs, and in order to permit the Bank to review cases involving
large amounts of financing, Bank approval would be required for sub-loans
as follows:
(a) the first two for beef processing;
(b) the first two for oilseed and milk processing;
(c) the first one of each sub-sector in the miscellaneous category;
(d) the first one of any PB which has not yet had a loan under
(a), (b), or (c) approved;
(e) those exceeding US$1.5 million; and
(f) those to an individual sub-borrower exceeding an aggregate limit
of US$2.0 million.
An assurance on Bank approval of such sub-loans was obtained during negotiations.



- 19 _
Sub-loan Terms and Conditions
5.14      Sub-loans would be limited to 80% of investment cost (excluding
the cost of land) and would have a maturity of up to 10 years, including a
maximum grace period of two years. The interest rate would be not less than
5%, with indexing based on ORTN prices. Since the ORTN prices are tied to the
general price index, the real rate of interest would also be 5% (the real
interest rate under the livestock loans--516-BR and 868-BR--and the grain stor-
age loan--857-BR--is about 3-1/4% 1/). PBs would pay BC 2-1/2% on refinancing,
also indexed on the ORTN prices, thus retaining a margin of 2-1/2%. Principal
and interest payments would be made semi-annually on the basis of the adjusted
balances outstanding. A commitment fee of 1/2% would be charged on the un-
disbursed sub-loan balances; an appraisal fee of 1% would be charged on the
amount of the investment cost of the sub-projects. Sub-loans would also be
subject to the financial tax of 1% of the initial loan amount, plus 1% of
interest payments.
5.15      Working capital financing to complement the financing of invest-
ment costs would be provided partly by sub-borrowers, partly by PBs, but
mainly by commercial banks. PBs would fill working capital financing requirements
not met by sub-borrowers and commercial banks (Schedule A).
E. Accounts and Auditing
e           State Development Banks
5.16      Participating state development banks' accounting methods are
satisfactory and their accounts are subject to inspection by BC. Each of
the participating state development banks has arranged with reputable audit
firms to conduct an audit of its 1972 accounts, which would be additional to,
and more comprehensive than, the BC inspections. It would be required that
each of the participating state development banks be subject to regular exam-
ination by external auditors, acceptable to the Bank, in respect of all their
activities (Schedule A).
5.17      The participating state development banks have not established
reserves for bad debts but rely on thorough appraisal and supervision and
security cover to protect themselves against default. However, although the
debt collection record of the participating state development banks to date
has been good (arrears are generally about 1% of portfolio), past experience
may not be a valid indication since their portfolios have expanded rapidly
in recent years and a large proportion of the loans are still in the grace
period. Consequently, participating state development banks would be required
to establish and maintain appropriate reserves for bad debts (Schedule A).
1/   Assuming a rate of inflation in the US of 4% per year.

Bank of Brazil
5.18      BB is also subject to inspection by BC.  These inspections would
be more detailed and would be given special focus for purposes of the project
(Schedule A).
Central Bank
5.19      The Project Account (para. 5.11) would be audited annually by an
independent auditing unit of the Monetary Council. A signed copy of the
auditor's report, including a description of the audit procedures, together
with certified copies of appropriate statements on the accounts, would be
forwarded to the Bank within four months of the end of the financial year.
Assurances on this were obtained during negotiations.
VI. PRODUCTION, MARKETING, PRICES, AND OPERATING RESULTS
A. Production
6.01      The result of investments in beef processing would be higher quality
beef, increased production for sale on the domestic and foreign markets, and
better utilization (-* by-products. If the Government's target of 500,000 tons
of beef exports by the end of the decade is reached (para. 3.08), the contri-
bution of project plants to exports of about 420,000 tons in the full devel-
opment year of 1978 would be about 240,000 tons, or 57%. Incremental output
resulting from the project would be about 190,000 tons, and incremental ex-
ports would be about 140,000 tons.
6.02      Rationalization of their operations would enable oilseed processors
to lower their costs of production and extract a higher percentage of oil
from the seeds. Annual incremental output at full development of plants in-
cluded in the project would be about 200,000 tons of meal and 40,000 tons of
oil, virtually all of which would be exported.
6.03      Project investments would result in an increase of milk processing
capacity at full development of approximately 440,000 liters/day, or about
3% of present capacity. Annual incremental output would amount to about 13
million liters of milk, 5,000 tons of milk powder, 3,000 tons of butter, and
6,000 tons of cheese.
6.04      Since little is known about the relative participation of various
types of enterprise in the miscellaneous export-oriented food and feed pro-
cessing category, the increase in capacity and production which would result
from investments in this component (it is anticipated that most sub-projects
would involve new plants) cannot be predicted.
.



- 21 -
B.  Marketing
6.05      The market for beef is strong.  Domestic demand is growing as a
result of continuing population growth although price increases and increas-
ed production of pork and poultry are expected to keep per capita beef con-
sumption approximately constant through the end of the decade (Annex 2).
In the international market, demand is expected to continue increasing at
a substantial rate, although nor m-atching the extremely high growth of the
past few years. Rising suppl should make markets more competitive in the
future, but Brazil's production costs are presently, and are likely to re-
main, favorable in comparison to those of other exporting countries and its
level of technology is generally 1igh so that beef processors should not
have difficulty in disposing of all of their exportable production.
6.06      Brazil exports beef primarily to North America and Europe.  Because
of the aftosa problem, only cooked beef is exported to the U.S. and Canada,
but European markets accept fresh beef as well. Investments under the project
would enable plants to meet export requirements and to broaden their export
markets. Products are exported direct to foreign wholesalers or via a foreign
or local sales agent. In the domestic market, sales are most often made
direct to numerous small butcher shops, although volume purchases by super-
markets are becoming a factor in some of the large urban areas.
6.07      Demand on the international market for soybean meal has increased
dramatically in recent years and is expected to exhibit a healthy increase
during the 1970's (Annex 3), but the demand for crude soybean oil is expected
to be rather sluggish throughout the decade. In the case of meal, no signi-
ficant marketing difficulties are expected because of the buoyant demand and
the availability of channels direct to foreign food companies or sales agents
(the domestic market is still quite small). The situation for oil is somewhat
more difficult, but foreign sales agents are available to handle this product
also.
6.08      The demand for pasteurized milk and milk products is increasing
rapidly, especially in the urban markets. The processors normally deliver
products directly to retail outlets. Fruit juices face a rising demand on
the world market, with contracts often being made directly with foreign
processors or distributors. Feed and processed food products which might be
produced under the project generally are faced with increasing demand and
attractive market prospects.
C. Prices
6.09      While international beef prices have shown a significant increase
over the last five years, demand and supply are expected to increase at about
equal rates through 1980, leaving prices at the end of the period about the



- 22 -
0
same as those obtained in 1972. Although domestic prices can be expected
to rise over the next few years in line with the Government's policy (para
3.08), the actual movements cannot be predicted with any degree of accuracy
and therefore constant prices were assumed for the financial analysis (see
Annex 10 for a list of financial and economic prices).
6.10      Almost all of the output of oilseed (soybean) processors would be
exported. Although demand for soybean products is expected to continue its
strong growth during the 1970's (para. 6.07), under certain circumstances
(which may not all materialize in fact), supply might increase even faster,
resulting in a conservative forecast of declines in price between 1972 and
1980 of about 13% for soybeans, of nearly 30% for soybean meal/cake, and of
17% for crude soybean oil (Annex 10).
6.11      Virtually all milk products would be sold on the domestic market.
For fresh milk, the current controlled price was used for the financial
analysis, and for butter and cheese, the current uncontrolled prices were
utilized. The current domestic price for powdered milk is above the interna-
tional price, but the domestic market is presently in a state of flux and
therefore the current world market price, projected to remain steady, was
used (Annex 10).

from 13 to 24% (Annexes 2 to 5). The investment, cash income, debt service,
and financial rate of return for a representative model for each project
component are indicated below (amounts in Cr$'000):

Sensitivity tests on all models indicate that the facilities would generate
adequate returns, even with increased costs or reduced operations. If invest-
ment costs were increased by 10%, the financial rates of return would be re-
duced to a range of 11% to 21X; if net income were reduced by 10%, the finan-
cial rates of return would fall to between 11% and 22%. Cash flow projections
demonstrate that sub-borrowers should be able to repay sub-loans within 10
years, including up to two years' grace, at 5% interest, plus ORTN indexing.
VII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
7.01      In assessing the project's economic feasibility, each major project
component has been separately analyzed to determine its internal economic rate
of return and its contributions to employment and the balance of payments.  On the
basis of these criteria, beef, oilseed, and milk processing all appear eco--
nomically justified.



- 24 -
7.02      Primary benefits from beef processing would stem from improvement
in the quality of beef, increase in production, and fuller utilization of
by-products vihile those from oilseed processing, which would mostly involve
turning small inefficient plants into larger efficient installations, would
principally take the form of net-value added and more efficient extraction
of oils and meal. Milk processing and miscellaneous food and feed processing
would generate primary benefits in the form of net value-added attributable
to the facilities financed under the project.
7.03      For the project as a whole, secondary benefits would accrue to input
suppliers, including farmers, civil works contractors, equipment suppliers
and workers employed by these groups, as well as to output users, consisting
mostly of the general consuming public. These secondary benefits, although
not included in the calculation of the project's internal rate(s) of return,
are substantial. Aside from the general secondary benefits, the project
would give added impetus to the progress of Espirito Santo, a state with a
very low degree of development, not unlike the Northeast.
7.04      Based on quantitative estimates of primary benefits and on real
costs involved, the most probable internal economic rates of return on project
components would be: (a) beef processing, 53%; (b) oilseed processing, 23%;
and (c) milk processing, 48% (Annex 10). If investment costs increased 15%,
the rates of return would vary from 8% for oilseed processing to 26% for
beef processing; if current operation costs, including raw material, increase
15%, the rates of return would range from negative for oilseed processing to
20% for beef processing; if gross benefits decreased 15%, the rates of return
would range from negative for oilseed processing to 10% for beef processing.            0
The latter rates are low because of the nature of processing industries with
their high throughout.
7.05      The project would provide employment during construction of about
20,000 man-years, or an average of about 4000 persons per year over five years.
At full development, project-financed facilities would provide new permanent
jobs for about 4,300 persons as a result of expansion of capacity and further
processing of products, especially beef.
7.06      The project would contribute to gross foreign exchange earnings about
US$176 million per year at full development, of which about US$160 million
would be contributed by the beef processing industry and about US$16 1/ mil-
lion by the oilseed processing industry. Net foreign exchange earnings would
increase by about US$169 million annually, US$154 million from beef processing
and US$15 million from oilseed processing. Milk processing, an import-sub-
stituting industry, would enable Brazil to save about US$23 million of
foreign exchange, gross, and about US$22 million net annually at full devel-
opment. Other food and feed processing industries participating in the proj-
ect would be essentially export-oriented, and increases in their output would
also enable Brazil to raise exports.
11   Assuming that only two-thirds of total output from project units are
exported.



- 25 -
7.07      The project would benefit a wide variety of people, from producers
to consumers, and it is not possible to specify the relative impact on various
groups. However, the project would generate revenues in the form of income and
value-added taxes of about Cr$139 million (US$23 million) annually at full
development.
7.08      Improvements in hygiene and. better utilization of by-products would
considerably reduce the existing beef processing plants' undesirable effects
upon the environment. Most new facilities to be financed under the project
would be located close to production areas where population density is very
low; hence, the need for safeguards against air pollution is minimal. Those
located in urban areas are generally covered by existing zoning laws which take
cognizance of such aspects as noise, odor and air pollutants. Adequate safe-
guards against water pollution would be provided for under the project through
requirement of adherence to existing regulations and, in the case of beef
processing, specific treatment of effluent.
7.09      As a whole, the project would serve as an important catalyst in
generating momentum for the agro-industrial sector in Brazil and thus pro-
vide the agricultural sector with expanding markets for primary agricultural
products and the consuming public with better quality goods at lower real
prices. In indirectly assisting the participating banks, it would improve
their ability and capacity to meet Brazil's continuing need for medium- and
long-term credit. The experience gained with this project would also be
important for other projects of a similar type in areas with a less-developed
institutional framework, such as the Northeast.
VIII.  AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATION
8.01      During loan negotiations agreement was reached on the following
principal points:
(a) the Government would permit domestic beef prices to rise
gradually to world market levels and would, in any case,
maintain domestic prices at levels which would provide
adequate returns to producers and processors (para. 4.04);
(b) the Central Bank would consult with the Bank on the qualifi-
cations and terms of reference of key staff for the Project
Team and would hire consultants as required for review of
sub-projects (para. 5.01); and
(c) the Central Bank would send feasibility studies and appraisals
of specified sub-projects to the Bank for approval of sub-
loans (para. 5.13).



- 26 -
8.02      Conditions of effectiveness of the loan would be:
(a)  that the Government had updated the beef export regula-
tions (para. 4.04); and
(b) that the Central Bank had signed a subsidiary loan agreement
with at least one PB incorporating the provisions of Schedule
A (para. 4.12), and had established the Project Team after
consultation with the Bank on key staff (para. 5.01).
8.03      A condition of disbursement against Central Bank refinancing of
sub-loan disbursements of each PB would be the signing of a subsidiary loan
agreement between BC and that PB incorporating the provis-ions of Schedule A
(para. 4.16).
8.04      The proposed project consitutes a suitable basis for a Bank loan
of US$54 million, for a term of 15 years, including a five-year grace period.
June 1, 1973

BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Lending Criteria
The following provisions would be included in the subsidiary loan
agreements signed between the Central Bank (BC) and the participating banks
(PBs) for implementation of the project:
Equity Participations
1.   In cases where the PB has an equity interest in the
applicant firm, arrangements satisfactory to BC would
be made for an independent appraisal of the proposed
sub-project.
2.   Project funds would not be made available to any PB if
(a) the aggregate of the PB's lending to, and-equity
participation in, any single enterprise exceeded
either of the following:
Mi) 25% of the PB's net worth, i.e., paid-up
equity plus free reserves; or
(ii) 50% of the total assets of an enterprise;
(these limitations would not apply to short-term
financing of seasonal working capital needs), or
(b) the equity investments of the PB in any single
enterprise exceeded 20% of the capital of the enter-
prise, except in extraordinary circumstances.
Reserves
3.   PBs would establish and maintain reserves for bad debts
by annual appropriations and at a level.deemed appropriate
by BC.
Auditin8
4.   Participating state development banks would be subject to
regular examination by external auditors acceptable to
the Bank in respect of all their activities, and a signed
copy of the auditors' report, together with certified copies



SCHEDULE A
Page 2
of annual accounts, would be forwarded to the Bank within
four months of the end of each financial year. In the course
of the examination, the auditors would make a specific review
of project accounts and would carry out spot checks of indi-
vidual sub-projects to ensure that project conditions were
being complied with. The auditors' report would describe
the review and checks undertaken.
5.   In the case of the Bank of Brazil, the following require-
ments on accounts and auditing would apply:
The Bank of Brazil (BB) will maintain separate
accounts at its headoffice for operations under
Lending Program, including disbursement documents
for each sub-loan, and will be subject to regular
annual audit on project accounts, including accounts
of branches involved in the Project by BC. A signed
copy of BC auditors' report, together with certified
copies of annual BB project accounts, will be for-
warded to the Bank within four months of the end
r BB's financial year. In the course of their
examination, BC's auditors will carry out spot
checks of individual beneficiaries to ensure
that project conditions are being complied with.
The auditors' report will describe the review
and checks undertaken.
Sub-loan Conditions
6.   Sub-loans would be limited to 80% of the investment costs (ex-
cluding the cost of land) of sub-projects and would be granted
for a term of up to 10 years, including a maximum of two years'
grace. They would carry an interest rate of 5%, plus ad-
justment based on the National Treasury Bond (ORTN) price,
and a commitment fee of 1/2% would be paid on undisbursed
loan balances. PBs would also charge an appraisal fee of
1% of the investment costs. Refinancing from BC would
carry an interest rate of 2-1/2% with ORTN indexing.
7.   PBs would meet short-term financing requirements for working
capital not filled by the sub-borrowers or the commercial
banks.
8.   PBs would £-eimburse BC according to the same repayment
schedules established for sub-borrowers.
9.   PBs would require a feasibility study to be undertaken for
each sub-project.  Any applicants considered by the PBs as



SCHEDULE A
Page 3
not having adequate expertise to prepare their own studies
would be required to engage consultants to perform them.
Banks would prepare a list of approved consultants for use
by the applicants.
10.   Feasibility studies would cover the technical, organizational,
financial and commercial aspects of sub-projects. Particular
attention would be paid to the following:
(a) inclusion of financial statements certified by auditors
approved by the PB in the case of sub-projects involving
the rehabilitation or extension of existing enterprises;
(b) compliance with all relevant regulations, especially
the environmental protection standards and, in the
case of beef and milk processing, the federal hygiene
regulations;
(c) inclusion of filtration or pondage system for treatment
of effluent in beef sub-projects;
(d) provision for future plant expansion in beef sub-
projects;
*                         (e)  detailed justification for the inclusion of refining
facilities in any oilseed sub-project based on special
factors particular to the sub-project;
(f) indication of export potential for sub-projects falling
in the miscellaneous food and feed category;
(g) inclusion of a component for appropriate training of
technical personnel in each sub-project;
(h) presentation of cash flow projections and calculation
of the financial rate of return;
(i) adequacy of the supply of raw materials and the demand
for final products, both domestic and foreign, the
analysis to include projections of both supply and
deman .
11. Appraisal of sub-projects by PBs would cover the same aspects
as those included in the feasibility studies. In addition,
the following points would be given particular emphasis:
(a) managerial competence of the applicants;



SCHEDULE A
page 4
(b)  financial condition of the applicants, including
the adequacy of their debt/equity ratio after the
inclusion of the proposed investment;
(c)  availability of sufficient working capital for the
applicants' present and future operations;
(d)  overall supply and demand situation for the industry,
taking into consideration present and planned capacity
within the PBs' respective spheres of operations and,
to the extent feasible, in other regions as well.
12.   PBs would hire consultants to evaluate the technical aspects
of sub-projects involving expertise not found among their
regular personnel. It is expected that this procedure would
be followed for many of the sub-projects.
13.   Procurement procedures would be decided upon by the sub-
borrowers with the concurrence of the PBs. As a general
rule, PBs would require that sub-borrowers obtain quotations
from at least three contractors or suppliers and submit
them to the PBs for review of quality, suitability, and
price of goods.
14.   PBs would disburse directly to suppliers except in extra-
ordinary circumstances.
15.   PBs would make quarterly inspection visits to sub-borrowers
during the implementation period and annual visits during
the remainder of the sub-loan term.
16.   PBs would ensure that all project beef and milk plants were
inspected by DIPOA.
17.   All oilseed processing sub-projects would involve the
adoption of solvent extraction techniques.

Beef Processing
A. Production
Livestock
1.        Brazil's cattle herd is estimated at about 84.8 million head, of
which about 77% is in the project area. The state with the largest herd is
Minas Gerais (19.5%), followed by Rio Grande do Sul (13.9%), Goias (11.0%),
Sao Paulo (10.3%), and Mato Grosso (9.5%), in which states abattoirs financed
under the project are expected to be mostly concentrated. In terms of func-
tional economic importance, about half of the country's as well as the proj-
ect area's total herd is beef cattle, 35% is both beef and dairy, and 15% is
dairy.  The southern states of Rio Grande do Sul, Parana, and Santa Catarina,
which are included in the project area,raise the largest proportion of their
herds for beef, namely, 68%. (Details on 1971 herd size, regional distribution,
and type are in Appendix 2-1.)
2.        Based on specialized activities, beef cattle farming in Brazil can
be classified into seven types -- breeding, raising, fattening, and combina-
tions of these three (Appendix 2-2). For Brazil as a whole, the combined
activity of breeding and raising constitutes the most important ranching ac-
tivity, but in the project area, the largest group of beef cattle producers
combine all three activities. Within the project area, breeding alone is
an important activity in the center-west states of Goias and Mato Grosso,
but less so in Minas Gerais, Sao Paulo, and other southeastern states, and
of almost no importance in the southern states. Fattening as a specialized
ranching activity in itself is only being conducted in the southeastern part
of the project area, mostly in the state of Sao Paulo.
3.        The average ratio of breeding cows to bulls is about 17 for Brazil
as well as for the project area. The region with the highest ratio is in the
project area, namely, the southeast, which includes Minas Gerais, Sao Paulo,
Rio de Janeiro, and Espirito Santo. Annual calving rates, expressed as the
ratio of annual births to stock of breeding cows, is about 52.7% for Brazil
and 53.5% for the project area. The ratios of cattle deaths due to diseases
and accidents to total cattle stock are about 3.8% for all of Brazil and 3.6%
for the project area. (Appendix 2-3 shows more details of cow/bull ratios,
calving rates, and mortality rates for 1971.)
4.        Virtually all cattle production in Brazil, including fattening op-
erations, is in pastures rather than in feedlots. There are about 147 mil-
*          lion ha of pasture land in all of Brazil, of which 73%, or 107.3 million ha,



ANNEX 2
Page 4
is in the project area.  These are mostly natural pastures and the south has
the highest proportion of natural pastures, with 89.5%. Only 27.4% of Brazil's
total is cultivated pasture and the proportion is even lower for the project
area, at 24.3%. Cattle density or stocking rate (head of cattle per ha of
pasture) for the country as a whole ranges from 0.5 (natural pasture during
dry season) to 2.9 (cultivated pasture during rainy season). The correspond-
ing range for the project area is 0.4-2.1. Details on distribution of pasture
areas as to region and type of pasture and stocking rates are in Appendix 2-4.
5.        Although Brazil has one of the world's largest cattle herds, its
productivity rates are very low compared to other principal beef-producing
countries. This law productivity is caused mainly by three factors: (a)
the poor health condition of the herd; (b) the low educational level of
farmers, which makes for the slow adoption of modern methods; and (c) the
"extractive" character of livestock exploitation in many areas, which means
that no special attention is paid to health, speed of growth, etc.
6.        In order to overcome these handicaps, the Government of Brazil has
been giving special emphasis to cattle production. Two IBRD Loans (516-BR
and 868-BR), totalling US$66 million, have been made to increase the produc-
tivity of cattle farming, and the proposed project is intended to improve
and expand the principal market for steers, hence, providing incentives for
higher farm production.
Beef
7.        Beef is produced chiefly in private commercial and municipal
slaughterhouses. The structure of the beef processing industry has grad-
ually shifted away from municipal and on-farm slaughtering operations to
private commercial units, mainly those without refrigeration facilities as
a result of continued consumer preference for warm meat (Appendix 2-5).
Overall beef production has grown from an annual average of 988,000 tons in
1952-1954 to 1.68 million tons in 1967-1969, or an annual compound growth
rate of 3.6%. Yield expressed in terms of carcass weight per head of cattle
slaughtered rose from an average 161 kg/head in 1952-1954 to 193 kg/head in
1967-69. The relative degree of sophistication among types of slaughter-
houses is indicated by differences in average weight of carcass for 1967-
1969, as follows: private commercial units with cold storage facilities,
228 kg/head; private commercial units without cold storage, 211 kg/head;
municipal slaughterhouses, 191 kg/head; and on-farm facilities, 170 kg/head.
8.        In terms of type of meat produced, the beef processing industry's
main output is fresh, unrefrigerated meat, which constituted about 71% of
total meat production in 1967-1969. Next in importance are refrigerated
fresh meat and charqui (dried beef), which made up 22.5% and 4.1%, respec-
tively; canned meat was only 1.2% of total production in these years
(Appendix 2-6). Average values per ton increased during the period 1954-
1956 to 1967-1969, with refrigerated meat registering the lowest rise at



ANNEX 2
Page 5
16.6% over the entire period (1.2% compound annual growth rate) and canned
meat the highest at 32.8% (2.2% compound annual growth rate). The average
unit values for the four meat types are as follows:
