9.        Despite its much slower rate of increase in average unit value,
refrigerated fresh meat has increased its share of total volume of meat pro-
duction from 13.9% in 1952-1954 to 22.5% in 1967-1969. Both unrefrigerated
fresh meat and charqui are declining, while canned meat is rising, in share
of total output (Appendix 2-6).
B.  The Industry
Structure
10.       Ownership.  Beef processing plants in Brazil are owned privately
by individuals, by corporations (Brazilian and foreign), by cooperatives and
by municipalities. Those owned by cooperatives of cattle producers represent
an integration of cattle producing, marketing, and processing. A number of
privately-owned and company-owned plants also include similar integration.
Those plants owned by foreign companies, however, include marketing of the
finished beef products in the integration. The ownership of plants by muni-
cipalities is declining, especially in areas where other plants can provide
beef for the domestic supply.
11.       Size.  The size of plants in Brazil is medium (200 to 800 cattle
per day) and small (50 to 200 per day). The large (1,000 to 2,000/day)
plants found in other countries apparently did not develop because a central
cattle marketing facility (stockyards) has never existed. While there are
still many very small plants (under 25/day), these will disappear as the
program of extending the national inspection service to all plants is im-
plemented, since most of them cannot comply with the national sanitary
standards or economically justify the investment necessary to achieve such
standards.
12.       Location.  There is a concentration of plants around population
centers and in areas where cattle have traditionally been fattened.  As



ANNEX 2
Page 6
cattle fattening areas are shifting and expanding into new areas, plants
are being planned for them. Cattle movement from the fattening ranch to
the plant is usually by truck, although some driving of cattle to truck
loading sites along improved roads does occur. Road improvements are under-
way or planned in many areas so that long distance movement of beef by re-
frigerated truck to port facilities for export is feasible. Rail transport
of cattle and beef products is also utilized in many areas, although rail
facilities are not being expanded into new areas.
13.       Expertise.  The expertise for the beef industry in Brazil is vested
in a small number of independent consultants, design engineers employed by a
number of abattoir operators, and engineers employed by the manufacturers of
processing and refrigeration equipment. The industry is also served by a
variety of consulting engineers who are primarily mechanical or civil en-
gineering oriented and make use of assistance given to them by the manufac-
turers of equipment. This is a pattern common in countries which have so-
phisticated beef industries.
Rgulations
14.       The beef processing industry is subject to regulation of sanitary
conditions urder the National Inspection Program, in existence for over 50
years. It is administered by the Division of Inspection of Products of
Animal Origin (DIPOA), one of six divisions in the National Department of
Animal Production in the Ministry of Agriculture.
15.       In 1971, a new law, No. 5760, was enacted establishing high stand-
ards of hygiene applicable to all meat processing plants. This law is being
implemented in a progressive manner starting in the southernmost state, Rio
Grande do Sul, with completion in that state planned by June 1973. This ac-
tion has resulted in closing several hundred substandard plants (mostly small
or very small) and the initiation of plans for rehabilitation and improvement
of many other plants. The next state scheduled for implementation of the law
is Sao Paulo, where preliminary surveys have been made and have already prompted
quite a number of plans for making improvements. In the case of export works,
plants must meet the sanitary requirements of importing countries and are
licensed by DIPOA when they so comply. 1/ In addition, importing countries
periodically send inspectors to review the operations and condition of plants
from which they receive supplies.
16.       All proposals to build new plants or improve or change existing
plants must be approved by DIPOA before any construction or equipment instal-
lations can begin. As work on the proposed changes nears completion, the
plant is reviewed by a DIPOA official and, unless the facility complies with
1/   Although the new domestic hygiene standards are generally comparable
to international standards, requirements of importing countries vary
widely.



ANNEX 2
Page 7
the approved drawings, it is not permitted to operate until required correc-
tions are made.
Plant Operations
17.       Because beef is highly perishable, the operation of a beef process-
ing plant involves some unique problems not encountered in other industries.
Once started, the operation must conform with limited time and temperature
conditions until the finished products are distributed from the plant or pur-
chased by the consumer.
18.       Seasonality.  Although some processing is conducted throughout the
year, there is a definite seasonal pattern with heavy slaughter during January
to May and limited slaughter during the other months. This corresponds with
the months when pasture is in the best condition and cattle achieve their
highest weight. Such seasonality of operations produces a number of ineffi-
ciencies such as: (a) less than 50% utilization of operating capacity during
seven months of the year; (b) necessity of retraining workers each year at
the start of the heavy slaughter season; and (c) necessity of storing a sub-
stantial inventory of meat each year to meet the uniform demand of the domes-
tic and export markets. While there is strong interest in starting feeding
lots in some areas so that cattle can be fattened throughout the year, it
will likely take five to 10 years to significantly change the existing fat-
tening pattern.
19.       Labor Use.  Beef processing plants are labor-intensive.  While mov-
ing chains for conveying carcasses and moving tables for conveying head,
viscera, meat and meat cuts, bones, and fat are being provided (and in some
cases required by DIPOA) in the improvement of plants, most of the work is
done by hand. Similarly, in processing the split beef carcass into quarters,
cuts, and boneless meat portions, most of the work of separating meat from
bone, cartilage, large blood vessels, and fat is also done by hand. Although
mechanical conveyors promote efficiency by moving products to and from the
workers, rapid and accurate knife work requires skill and training.
20.       Hygiene.  On the basis of observation of sample plants, hygienic
conditions are considered to be below internationally accepted standards
with few exceptions, and many structures have deteriorated so that they can-
not be maintained in acceptable sanitary condition. Many plants lack accept-
able drainage systems and separation between areas where edible products are
prepared and areas where inedible by-products are handled, and sanitary fa-
cilities for employees are usually missing or inadequate. The design and
construction of most equipment is inadequate because it is difficult or
impossible to clean and sanitize thoroughly and allows contamination of
edible products by contact with inedible or diseased material. Where new
construction has been undertaken or new equipment installed, most of these
deficiencies have been corrected.
21.       Effluent Disposal.  There is considerable variation in the treat-
ment of effluent.  Some plants use filtration or pondage systems but others



ANNEX 2
Page 8
discharge waste into streams or rivers either directly or after passing it
through a simple interceptor trap. The latter cases pose a health hazard
to users of water downstream, although this problem is less likely to occur
when there is a local sewerage treatment plant.
22.       Profitability.  Maximum profitability in the beef processing opera-
tion requires that all parts of the animal be utilized in preparing market-
able products of highest value and the recent upgrading of the national stand-
ards has resulted in closing many plants that were not doing so. Improved
plants often include facilities for preparing beef cuts and boneless beef,
which have a higher value than beef carcass sides or quarters. In some
plants, facilities are planned for preparing frozen cooked meat and/or canned
meat, which have even higher value. Improvements are also planned to allow
processing inedible parts of the carcass. Valuable protein materials needed
for animal feeds and other materials from these parts which have considerable
value for industrial use will be saved and upgraded in quality.
23.       Management.  The management and supervision of meat processing
plants appear to be about average in comparison with conditions in other major
producing countries. Since most of the plants are undergoing substantial im-
provement and changes in operations, management personnel will need training
in new processing techniques and the higher standards of hygiene. DIPOA
veterinarians can arrange for training necessary to assist management in
complying with the national and "special" standards of foreign countries,
and equipment suppliers provide training in the use of new equipment, new
processing techniques, and the preparation of new products for domestic and
export markets. In some cases, firms send their personnel to plants in other
countries for training.
Inspection Program
24.       Operations.  Routine post-mortem inspections are being performed
in a manner that would meet internationally accepted standards, and the vet-
erinary inspection of diseased or otherwise abnormal carcasses is also satis-
factory. However, there is considerable variation in the application of reg-
ulations regarding the sanitary condition of facilities, equipment, and
operations. This situation is by no means unique to Brazil, but DIPOA will
make efforts to achieve a greater degree of uniformity.
25.       Cost.  The cost of inspection in beef processing plants is about
30% of DIPOA's US$12 million annual budget, and the Government is now con-
sidering a "fee per head inspected" basis of financing at least a part of
the program. The fee would be Cr$3 to Cr$6 per head for beef (about 1/2%
of the price per head).
Investment Needs
26.       Investment needs will be substantial for most plants.  In a number
of cases, the most practical way to meet the required hygiene standards will



ANNEX 2
Page 9
be to build a new plant. If the present plant can be improved, the cost will
be less but will still be substantial because, in many cases, it will mean
removing existing inadequate equipment and structures and rebuilding and re-
equipping the plant. Some plants will be upgraded to meet the new domestic
*          standards only.  While these are comparable to international standards, the
plants may produce less processed products (chilled quarters) than they would
if they were producing for the international market. Other plants which will
be upgraded to meet export standards will produce more fully-processed prod-
ucts (e.g., frozen quarters and cuts), in general, and will require at least
an extra boning room, a freezing tunnel, and frozen storage.  Additional in-
vestment will be made in increasing the capacity of some plants and in in-
stalling facilities to prepare more fully-processed products (cooked, canned,
special cuts, etc.).
Sources and Availability of Funds
27.       Beef processing firms have had to rely heavily on the internal gen-
eration of funds to meet investment needs since the availability of long-term
financing is extremely limited. The state development banks constitute the
primary external source of long-term funds for the industry, but even they
have not yet become involved to an appreciable extent. In some cases, firms
have financed long-term needs by rolling over short-term loans. Then, too,
there is a difference between foreign-owned and Brazilian-owned firms:  the
0>         former are often able to obtain funds from the parent company or other sources
abroad, while the latter must rely on domestic sources. In any case, the
traditional sources are totally inadequate to meet the needs imposed by the
implementation of the new hygiene regulations.
28.       Working capital requirements are high for this type of industry,
although they are less in Brazil than in some other countries since payment
for cattle is usually made about 30 days after slaughter. These needs are
met with internally-generated funds and with short-term financing from the
commercial banks, including the Bank of Brazil.
Equipment Supply
29.       The range of equipment manufactured in Brazil is comprehensive and
reflects the way in which the manufacturers keep themselves informed on de-
velopments in other parts of the world,as well as the collaboration that
exists between overseas manufacturers and the local industry in the form of
joint ventures and facilities to manufacture under license. The firms' ca-
pacity to keep up with the current rate of development in the meat industry
is adequate and they have considerable reserve, particularly in regard to
refrigeration and other ancillary services. The times given for supply and
installation of processing equipment was generally less than that normally
found in other countries, largely due to the intensive on-site work done as
against maximum factory fabrication in more developed countries.

Page 10
C. Marketing
Domestic Market
30.       Consumption Data.  Apparent domestic consumption of the four prin-
cipal types of beef - unrefrigerated fresh, refrigerated fresh, canned and
charqui - grew from about 1.10 million tons in 1960 to 1.54 million tons in
1969, or an annual growth rate of 3.9%. Refrigerated fresh beef showed the
largest growth, at 8.5% per year, followed by unrefrigerated fresh beef (2.1%
annual growth) and canned beef (1.9% annual growth). Consumption of charqui,
on the other hand, declined at an annual rate of 2.7% between 1961 and 1969.
Unrefrigerated fresh beef constituted about 75% of the four principal beef
types in 1966-69, reaching an annual level of 1.1 million tons in 1969.
Refrigerated fresh meat made up 21% of the 1966-69 totals, with a level of
369,000 tons in 1969.
31.       Urban and Rural Markets.  The rural market is estimated to make
up about 40% of the total beef market in Brazil.  It is currently supplied
by small, non-inspected plants and almost all beef is sold to the retailer
as bone-in fresh sides or quarters, unprocessed, except for basic cleaning.
The retailer then breaks down the carcass according to local preferences.
The utilization of beef and edible offal is quite high, but losses never-
theless occur because no further processing is done; inedible by-products
are usually not utilized. Unreported slaughter supplements the small plant/
local butcher supply system.
32.       In the urban market, significant changes are occurring in the prod-
uct mix offered. Although less than 20% of the urban retail trade is now
through self-service supermarkets, a rapid rate of growth has resulted in
great improvements in chilled and frozen beef merchandizing through such
outlets. In addition to this widening acceptance of chilled and frozen
beef, inhabitants of the larger cities are also accepting various types of
pre-processed beef such as sausages, vacuum-packed, cooked frozen, and canned
beef products. Witness to the change in taste are the considerable numbers
of relatively small specialized plants which have sprung up during the last
decade. The large slaughterhouses in or near the major cities also manufac-
ture and market these products. For the present, the development of ready
meats, frozen T.V. dinners, and boil-in-bag are in the test market stage,
but some of the large slaughterhouses expect to go into regular production
of limited quantities by 1974. The bulk of the urban market, however, is
still dominated by a consumer preference for unchilled meat and deals with
small retail butchers.
33.       Storage and Transport.  In 1969 about 426,000 tons of meat, or ap-
proximately 18% of total meat production, went through cold storage. Present
static cold storage capacity for meat in Brazil is about 70,000 tons, out of
a total of 100,000 tons for all products, including private as well as public
fa-ilities. A rapid expansion will raise static capacity to about 170,000
tons by 1974, 37,800 tons of which will be exclusively for export purposes.



ANNEX 2
Page 11
However, at a turnover rate of seven times a year, about 900,000 tons of
meat will go through storage, equal to about 28% of projected total meat
production, and more facilities will be needed to serve the domestic (ca-
pacity must be sufficient to cover requirements during the low beef produc-
tion season: June through December) and export markets.
34.       Compared to other regions of Brazil, the center-south has a rela-
tively well-developed transport system. The rate of expanding and improving
major highways has been very rapid in recent years, and the programs are being
continued under a master plan, partially assisted by the Bank. The rural road
system is also receiving increased attention, with a program to develop 10,000
km of roads begun recently. The railroad system, important to beef transport
in Sao Paulo state particularly, is also receiving increased attention. The
Government is arranging the purchase of additional refrigerated railroad cars,
and rail line improvement is being carried out, especially on the Sao Paulo
to Santos line.
35.       In 1969 there were about 800 insulated and mechanically refrigerated
trucks operating in Brazil. However, many of the latter are used solely for
export shipments; most of the meat destined for the domestic market is shipped
in insulated trucks, which results in a higher rate of product quality deteri-
oration and spoilage than necessary. Because of the shift in slaughterhouse
locations to fattening areas, it is important that mechanically refrigerated
a         truck fleets be used to keep the beef from spoiling.
Export Market
36.       Export Data.  While world imports of beef rose from about 1.94 mil-
lion tons in 1965/66 to 2.61 million tons in 1970/71 (about 6% per year),
Brazil's beef exports increased sharply from 30,000 tons valued at US$20
million in 1966 to 174,000 tons (about 34% per year) at US$200 million in
1972. The rise in unit value was from about US$670/ton in 1966 to US$1,150/ton
in 1972. The most important beef export is frozen beef, with a 1970 total of
about 93,000 tons, valued at US$63.2 million, compared with canned beef, the
second most important category, which had a 1970 volume of less than 17,000
tons, valued at US$15.8 million. Chilled beef exports are still rather small,
with a 1970 volume of just over 4,000 tons, valued at US$5.5 million (Appen-
dix 2-8).
37.       Beef exports still account for a small percentage of domestic pro-
duction. Both in 1970 and 1971, they accounted for only about 8.4% of pro-
duction and if we separate fresh (both refrigerated and unrefrigerated) beef
from canned beef exports, an even more interesting picture emerges. Between
1966 and 1969, fresh (frozen and chilled) meat exports accounted for only
2.0% of total domestic production of fresh meat. Canned meat exports, on
the other hand, accounted for 70.0% of total canned meat production (Appen-
dix 2-8).
0



ANNEX 2
Page 12
38.       Volume of frozen beef exports grew at 24% annually between 1960-
1961 and 1969-70, with average price per ton (FOB Brazilian ports) corres-
pondingly rising from US$502 to US$636. Chilled beef exports grew in volume
at 55% per year during the same period, with average price per ton increasing
from US$455 to US$823. Canned beef registered the lowest growth in export
volume as well as in average prices received during this period; volume grew
at 4.4% per year and price per ton changed from US$847 to US$910. Details
on quantities, values, and prices of beef exports are shown in Appendix 2-8,
which also presents data on charqui and veal exports. Comparison between
FOB export price and average price received by processors are shown in
Appendix 2-9.
39.       Importing Countries.  Brazil now exports to almost every Common
Market and other European countries, Israel, and the US, with small quanti-
ties also going to other Middle East countries, the Caribbean, and Japan
(Appendix 2-10). In 1970-71, 68% of Brazil's beef exports weie fresh
(chilled or frozen) and 32% were cooked. Western Europe took more than
75% of the fresh beef, with Italy, the low countries, Spain, and the UK
taking more than 5,000 tons each, and Greece, Federal Republic of Germany,
Portugal, and Switzerland taking more than 2,000 tons each. The US and
Canada took more than 75% of all cooked beef exports. Outside these re-
gions, Israel is the only other large market, buying nearly 6,000 tons in
1970-71. Demand in thf'se countries increased rapidly during the 1960's -
by 25% in the US, by 20% in the Common Market countries, and by between 200%
and 400% in several southern European countries.
40.       Packaging for export is undergoing substantial changes to lessen
damage and spoilage in transit. Cardboard cartons with dividers are replac-
ing wooden boxes for packing canned products and special cuts, and polyethylene
bags and cardboard cartons are fast being substituted for hessian and mesh
stocking in the packaging of bone-in frozen and chilled meat.
41.       Sales and Marketing Procedure.  Export sales are handled in three
ways: direct sales, sales via a foreign agent, or sales via a Brazilian
agent. The large and well-established slaughterhouses sell directly to for-
eign wholesalers, either through a Brazilian-based export sales team or,
where volume warrants, through branch sales offices abroad. In addition,
some sales are made to foreign agents.
42.       Sales to foreign agents or Brazilian agents are the normal channel
of distribution for the smaller exporting slaughterhouses, which do not have
strong international contacts. Agents' fees are understood to be in the re-
gion of 6 to 7%, sometimes ranging up to 15% of net sales. The fees vary
according to the level of services provided.
43.       Marketing support is of three varieties:  that provided by the
manufacturers' associations, that provided by one of the State Goverrnents,
and that provided by the Federal Government. One of the associations, the
Br--.ilian Committee of Meat Exporters (CBEC), operates marketing support or-
ganizations in Rio Grande do Sul, Sao Paulo, and Minas Gerais/Espirito Santo,



ANNEX 2
Page 13
and there are plans to extend services to Goias and Mato Grosso in the near
future.  The only marketing support offered at the state level is provided
in Sao Paulo, where the Export Promotion Center (COPEME) has been in existence
for a year. It works closely with the state development bank, and its objec-
tive is to directly assist private manufacturers and commodity exporters and
to do long-range research studies. Federal Government support is provided
primarily through CACEX, the export arm of the Bank of Brazil, which provides
market intelligence services and export financing to processors.
44.       Major port improvement programs are now underway in Vitoria (ES),
Santos (SP), and Rio Grande (RS) and cold storage capacity is being expanded.
Static cold storage facilities at the ports should increase from 11,200 tons
in 1972 to 52,800 tons in 1974, of which 15,000 tons at Santos will be used
specifically for fruit juice storage. This will provide adequate facilities
for throughput of about 450,000 tons per year for beef and 180,000 tons of
juice (at a turnover of 12 times a year), if required and result in more
rapid turnaround times for both ships and trucks delivering to the port.
It will also eliminate the need for direct loading from truck to ship, which
is a common occurrence now in the ports of Santos and Rio Grande.
45.       Product Mix.  The variety of beef products exported now by Brazil
is limited because of the prevailing price structure of the international
market and the varying foreign sanitation regulations. Brazilian slaughter-
houses have concentrated on building up exports of frozen and chilled quarters
and sides to markets such as Italy, Spain, and Holland, where sanitary regula-
tions are less stringent. The US and Canada, however, prohibit the importa-
tion of uncooked meats from countries which have not eradicated foot-and-mouth
disease so that Brazilian processors have developed cooked products which
meet specific needs of the hotel, restaurant, and institutional market for
the US and they have also been well accepted in Canada.
46.       For the future, Government is concerned about increasing the amount
of value added in beef exports so as to derive greater economic advantage for
the country as a whole. Greater concentration on processed beef products and
special cuts will be necessary anyway if Brazil is to become a major world ex-
porter, for these are the market growth areas of the 1970's and this is the
best way for Brazil to make a significant impact on the important markets of
the Federal Republic of Germany and the UK. New market areas for frozen
cooked beef have been identified in the rapidly growing hotel, restaurant
and institutional market and also in pre-portioned products. New market
areas for non-cooked beef have been identified as "cryovac" packs (vacuum
packaging in plastic) and prepacked special cuts. While there are diffi-
culties with the latter because considerable variations of the special cuts
exist from country to country and even from region to region, both get premium
prices, require no further processing, and have adequate shelf lives.
47.       Trade Barriers.  Specific and "ad valorem" duties are not the major
obstacles to expansion of international trade in meat since their incidence
is not unreasonably high and rarely could they be considered prohibitive.



ANNEX 2
Page 14
Governments exhibit an increasingly flexible approach towards the use of
tariffs, and sliding-scale duties are now used by the EEC, Austria, and
Sweden. Sliding-scale duties allow flexibility: duties are lifted when
the local market price is above a fixed guide price and a levy equal to
the difference between the market and guide price is reimposed when the
market price falls below the guide price.
48.       Under normal circumstances, Brazilian beef entering the EEC is
treated as third country beef and is therefore subject to a basic 16% "ad
valorem" duty as well as to the sLiding-scale duty. The UK maintains a 5%
"ad valorem" duty on beef and veal imports from non-preference areas, of
which Brazil is one, and this level of duty will continue until the UK be-
gins to move towards the EEC Common External Tairiff on January 1, 1974. The
US does not employ tariffs to control meat imports although countries that
may supply uncooked meats are subject to individual national quotas allocated
on the basis of previous performance (these quotas are temporarily suspended
at the present time). There is no control on imports of cooked beef.
49.       Non-tariff barriers raise more serious obstacles to expansion.
Import quotas, seasonal limitations, licensing, hygienestandards, and con-
trol procedures and tariff classification methods complicate the marketing
problems of an international meat exporter, largely because of the profusion
of various national st_ndards and applications of those standards.  However,
Brazilian meat exporting companies do not feel taht non-tariff barriers are
insurmountable, but rather that the situation has eased in recent years and
that adjustment to non-tariff barriers is a relatively mechanical process,
which does not now upset their trading patterns very much.
Government Policy
50.       Obiectives - Brazil's two most important objectives with signi-
ficant bearing on beef policy are: (a) continued increase in non-traditional
exports in order to raise foreign exchange earnings and stabilize trade with
other countries; and (b) reduction in the rate of inflation to a 1973 target
level of 12%, which would be the lowest in well over a decade. With reference
to the first objective, Brazil aims at exporting about 0.5 million tons of
beef by 1980, from the current level of less than 200,000 tons. As a low-
cost beef producer and with average per-capita income substantially lower
than those of major beef importing countries, Brazil could be expected to
be a natural beef exporter. Indeed, it would be currently exporting far
more than the 1980 target if this did not run counter to the second objective
of keeping inflation in check. The importance of this second objective under-
scores the reason for close Government regulation over beef exports and do-
me8tic beef prices.
51.       Due to the importance of the domestic beef price in the general
price index, the Government has therefore found it necessary to control this
price as part of its inflation control program. Thus, the 1972 domestic whole-
saie price of beef was set at only about 60% of the corresponding FOB export
price (about US$ 700/ton as compared to US$1,150/ton for deboned beef).  To



ANNEX 2
Page 15
sustain this price differential as well as assure adequate returns to pro-
*         cessors, the Government has also established maximum prices to farmers and
adopted a set of export controls. These controls include an export tax, a
system of xport quotas, and a storage requirement tied to exported quanti-
ties, deoigned to even out seasonal beef supply to the domestic market (see
paras 58-62). Between now and 1980, the Government intends to allow the
domestic price to rise gradually to a level consistent with the correspond-
ing FOB export price. This goal of allowing price to increase continuously
is consistent with the price pattern of the last several years. Real prices
received by producers in January 1973 were 60% higher than those in January
1970, an average annual rise of about 17%. In order to close the gap between
domestic and FOB export price for 1980, the domestic price has to rise at an
average of 6.6% per year faster than FOB export price. The extent of the
required real rise would, of course, depend on the behavior of the interna-
tional price of beef.
52.       A further important element in the situation and one which has a
substantial influence on investment demand under the project, is the Govern-
ment's drive to raise the hygiene standards of all meat processing plants.
The objective is to upgrade the quality of beef sold on the domestic market
to international standards. In addition to supplying high quality beef to
domestic consumers, beef processors would be operating at a level of sanita-
tion which would permit them to export if they had the facilities to produce
the types of products demanded in the international market.  New federal sani-
tary regulations were promulgated at the end of 1971 and are being applied to
all meat processing plants on a state-by-state basis.
53.       Production Increases.  The Government has undertaken a number of
programs to increase the production of beef, given the various price con-
straints. One of the important initiatives is the National Animal Health
Program, particularly the Aftosa (foot-and-mouth disease) Control Program.
This program, directed by the Ministry of Avriculture, is funded by the Gov-
ernment and also by a loan from the Inter-American Development Bank, which
is financing the establishment of laboratories for the quality control of
vaccines. The current planning for aftosa control envisions bringing all
of the principle beef producing states under full vaccination control within
16 years of its initiation in 1971. The aftosa control program has high
priority because the disease affects hundreds of thousands of cattle in
Brazil and, although mortality is not great, cattle weight and calving
rates are adversely affected to a serious degree.
54.       The Government is also implementing a livestock development pro-
gram with the assistance of financing from the Bank (516-BR and 868-BR) and
IDB. Long-term credit is made available to ranchers for investments designed
to increase the size and productivity of their herds, in combination with
technical assistance on pasture establishment and herd management. Included
in the program is applied research aimed at developing improved methods of
pasture establishment, maintenance, and utilization. A further element is
a marketing study, currently underway, which seeks to identify improvements



ANNEX 2
Page 16
needed in the procedures and institutional framework for the marketing of
livestock and meat products. The program is administered by a special Gov-
erment agency, CONDEPE, which operates under the umbrella of the Ministry
of Agriculture.
55.       Another policy aimed at increasing the national herd prohibits
processors from slaughtering female bovines of breeding age (up to six
years) unless the animals are certified as being diseased or otherwise
physically incapable of breeding. Finally, various fiscal incentives are
offered ranchers to encourage them to improve their productivity.
56.       Domestic Demand.  Domestic demand is the most uncertain element in
the equation. Rising population and per capita income would tend to cause
an increase in consumption. However, neither the price of beef nor the
prices of substitute goods (primarily pigs and poultry) vill remain constant.
The price of beef will rise, in line with the Government's goal of equalizing
the domestic and international prices, while the prices of pigs and poultry
are likely to decline in real terms as supply expands at a fairly rapid pace.
If the real price of beef increases sufficiently in relation to the real
prices of pigs and poultry, the income and price effects will offset each
other, per capita domestic demand will remain constant, and increases in
per capita production -an be exported. The Government's target of 500,000
tons of beef exports by the end of the decade then is likely to be attained.
57.       Control Mechanisms.  There must, in any case, be a restriction on
exports as long as the domestic price for beef is below the world market
price. Furthermore, if supply and demand do not quite match at the price
which the Government considers acceptable, some form of price controls will
be needed. Thus, export quotas and price controls, together with a storage
requirement designed to counteract the seasonal fluctuation in supply, are
the main features of the Government's effort to regulate the flow of beef
to the domestic and foreign markets. Two new elements for 1973 include an
export tax and a decrease in the ICM.
58.       The export quota systems for 1972 and 1973 are indicated in Appen-
dix 2-11. For each of the years, the overall quota is divided into an untied
quota, a quota related to storage requirements, and a special quota reserved
for new processors. Furthermore, each beef processing firm must supply the
domestic market with a proportion of its slaughterings which meets or exceeds
established standards (50% in Rio Grade Do Sul and 60% in Central Brazil).
Between 1972 and 1973, the overall quota was reduced from 180,000 tons to
144,500 tons (actual exports in 1972 were about 174,000 tons). The untied
quota was reduced from 55,000 tons to 35,000 tons, the quota related to
storage requirements was reduced from 113,000 to 69,000 tons, and the quota
for new processors was increased slightly from 12,000 to 18,000 tons. The
storage requirements were increased for 1973. In 1972, the requirements
were 1 ton of beef placed in storage for every 5 tons exported (Rio Grande
do Ful) and 1 ton stored to 1.5 tons exported (Central Brazil).  For 1973,



ANNEX 2
Page 17
they are 2:5 (Rio Grande do Sul) and 1:1 (Central Brazil). 1/ At the time
of the appraisal mission (November/December 1972), the Government was plan-
.         ning to raise the overall quota for 1973 to 203,000 tons, primarily through
an increase in the untied quota. A brief interlude of freed consumer prices
for beef at the beginning of this year led to increases of about 40% over the
previous agreed prices, however, and the Government subsequently reduced the
quota for 1973.
59.       The Government also introduced a new package of agreed prices 2/
and taxes in response to the experience with freed prices. The price of
steers was set at about Cr$4.20/kg 3/ (down from an unregulated price of about
Cr$4.67/kg); the agreed wholesale prices for forequarters and hindquarters
were raised from Cr$3.20 and Cr$4.20 to Cr$3.50 and Cr$5.00, respectively;
the ICM (value-added) tax on cattle and beef products was decreased from
about 15% to 5%; and an export tax on beef products of US$200/ton was in-
stituted.
60.       The net effect of all these changes was to lower slightly the
price to producers (the decrease in sales price was largely offset by the
cut in the ICM), increase the margin to processors on domestic sales, de-
crease the margin to processors on export sales, increase the (official)
price to consumers, and, of course, decrease exports between 1972 and 1973.
Furthermore, the Government has indicated that the states will receive at
least a part of the proceeds from the export tax as partial compensation
for the loss of revenue from the decrease in the ICM (a state tax).  While
the export tax thus may have been instituted to recover some of the tax rev-
enues lost, the reduction in exports appears to have been largely a symbolic
gesture. Since exports constitute less than 10% of total production, the
reduction should not have a major influence on the domestic price.
61.       Until domestic and world prices are the same, however, the Govern-
ment will be faced with the necessity of juggling a series of export controls
and price agreements in an attempt to meet domestic demand at the artifically
low price while avoiding a negative effect on production. The Government has
1/   The combined result, for any given export processor, of the requirement
on slaughterings and the requirement on storage is as follows (1973):
in Rio Grande do Sul, the processor supplies 50% of his meat output
directly to the domestic market, stores 25% of the remainder, and ex-
ports the rest; in Central Brazil, the processor supplies 60% to the
domestic market, stores 50% of the remainder, and exports the rest.
Thus, in Rio Grande do Sul, 50% of the meat output goes directly to
the domestic market, 12.5% goes into domestic storage, and 37.5% is
exported. In Central Brazil, 50% goes directly to the domestic market,
25% goes into domestic storage, and 25% is exported.
2/   At the present time, prices on cattle and wholesale prices are maintained
through informal agreements between the Government and processors. Re-
tailers are generally permitted a margin of 40 to 50%.
3/   Carcass weight.



ANNEX 2
Page 18
assured the Bank that it will permit the domestic price of beef to rise,
although not precipitously, in real terms until it reaches the world
market price. At that level, it is likely that Brazil will be a major
exporter, without any controls.
D. Financial Models
62.       Five models have been selected as representative examples for the
beef sector. Four of the models represent varying sized beef processing
plants likely: to be financed by the project. The fifth represents a refri-
gerated trucking operation.
Beef Processing Facilities
63.       The primary stimulus to investment in beef processing facilities
is the necessity of meeting new federal hygiene requirements. A few of the
enterprises which would participate in the project may already satisfy the
sanitary regulations and may simply install additional facilities such as
cooking units for the further processing of beef products, but most would
have to make some modifications in order to comply with the regulations.
Secondly, there would je a number of plants wishing to make modifications
which would enable them to move into the export market.  Finally, some firms
will be increasing the capacity of their plants and constructing some new
plants in order to accommodate the slaughtering volume of plants closed by
the hygiene officials and the additional production from the country's grow-
ing herd.
64.       In light of the above factors, the primary variables among plants
which would be included in the project are capacity, intended market and
product line. Plants in Brazil are generally in the small to medium range
and can perhaps be represented by three capacities: 200 head/day, 400 head/
day, and 800 head/day. Most of the plants at 200 head/day probably will pro-
duce only for the domestic market, while those at 800 head/day almost cer-
tainly will export as much of their production as possible, limited only by
the Government's export restrictions. Some of the plants at 400 head/day
would export, others would not. In order to maintain a conservative approach,
it is assumed that 20% of the beef production of export plants would be sold
on the international market, a figure which is based on current exports and
the proportion of export-oriented plants likely to participate in the project.
The diversity of products produced by beef processing plants is very large,
and therefore, basic products are assumed for each market in order to achieve
a common denominator: warm and chilled quarters for the domestic market and
frozen quarters and cuts for the export market. Analyses of cooking units
and charqui (dried beef) operations have been performed, and the rates of
return are high, since the marginal investments are relatively small.
6,.       The models, then, consist of the following:
(a) Model A: capacity 200 head/day, production for the
domestic market;



ANNEX 2
Page 19
(b)  Model B:  capacity 400 head/day, production for the
domestic market;
(c)  Mocel C:  capacity 400 head/day, production for the
domestic and export markets; and
(d)  Model D:  capacity 800 head/day, production for the
domestic and export markets.
66.       Prices used for the models are those that existed in November 1972.
Since the changes introduced at the beginning of 1973, involving the decrease
in the price of cattle, the increase in the agreed domestic price for beef, the
reduction in ICM, and the imposition of the export tax, led to an increase in
the average margin to processors, the models were not revised to reflect the
new situation. WTorld market prices are expected to remain approximately con-
stant during the life of the project, and domestic prices are also taken to be
constant, even though they are expected to rise gradually to world market levels.
67.       For the financial analysis, total revenue and operating costs of
plants are taken as incremental. This procedure is adopted since almost all
sub-projects would involve modifications to meet the federal hygiene stand-
ards, and if the plants did not comply with the regulations, they could not
operate. Therefore, the revenue and operating costs in the "without project"
case are zero.
68.       Similarly, incremental investment costs are assumed, in each case,
to be the cost of a new plant. Existing facilities would have a value, even
if they were not modified to meet the hygiene regulations (and thus could not
operate), since other investors would be willing to purchase them, make the
necessary modifications, and run the plants. The price the investors would
be willing to pay depends on the depreciated value of existing facilities and
the cost of modifications necessary to meet the standards. The significance
of depreciated facilities for the rate of return calculation is that replace-
ment would be required sooner than for new facilities; the maximum amount
investors would pay would be determined by the cost of a new plant less the
cost of required modifications. Therefore, new plant investment costs are
assumed in all cases.
Refrigerated Trucking Company
69.       Model E represents a small enterprise investing in five refrigerated
trailers with tractors. The main business of the company, working in close
connection with a few slaughterhouses, is to transport deep frozen meat to
the harbors for export. It is assumed that the trailers get at least an
average load of other products on each third trip back, resulting in a total
utilization of the fleet of about 70%. Nominal capacity of the trailers is
25 tons and the average load is assumed to be 22 tons. The refrigeration
equipment is capable of maintaining temperature in the trailers at -20°C.



ANNEX 2
Page 20
70.       The tariff used in the model is that which existed in November
1972. The operating costs are calculated from information received during
the field visits and from the Brazil Transport Sector Brief (Supplement IV,
January 1973). The investment costs are based on quotations for tractors
and trailers and include a small office, garage, and repair shop. Three
truck/trailer units would be purchased the first year and two the second.

E. Investment Demand Under the Project
71.       The amount of long-term financing to be provided under the project
is based on estimates of investment needs over the project period. Estimates
were made by the consultants who prepared the project, in conjunction with
the Central Bank. On the basis of a sector survey and field analysis, they
estimated that the total investment requirements for beef processing are
Cr$2.2 billion (US$365 million). Taking into consideration these overall
needs, the consultants prepared a schedule of investments which were l:ikely
to be suitable for inclusion in the project and which were apt to be sub-
mitted to the participating banks for financing. This schedule totals
Cr$656 million (US$108 million), which is the amount included in the pro-
posed project.
72.       The consultants also compared their estimates with the investment
demand over the project period, as seen by the participating banks. Although
the requirement for beef processing, as measured by the banks (Cr$512 million),
is almost 25% less than that projected by the consultants (Cr$656 million), the
latter included amounts for cold storage and refrigerated transport, while
the figure presented by the banks is essentially for processing facilities.
Furthermore, when the data were collected in the second half of 1972, DIPOA
had completed its survey (to determine whether or not plants comply with the
new sanitary standards) only in the state of Rio Grande do Sul and was in
the process of completing its survey in Sao Paulo. Therefore, it would be
expected that the demand for long-term financing would increase as the sur-
veys were completed in each of the states and processors were informed of
the modifications that would be necessary to comply with the regulations.
(DIPOA approved 153 plans for plant modification or construction during
1972, with only the survey in Rio Grande do Sul having been completed.)
The consultants' projections take this factor into account.
73.       The following are project investments estimated by the consultants
and by the participating banks for the project period:
Consultants    Participating Banks

BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Oilseed Processing
A. Production
Seed
1.        Soybean.  Soybeans would be the most important raw material input
for project-financed oilseed processing facilities. They have emerged as one
of the most successful farm crops, in Brazil, both in terms of production
growth and in profitability to the farmer. Annual farm output grew at about
19% per year between 1952-54 and 1969-71, rising from a yearly average of
about 945,000 tons in the earlier years to about 1.5 million tons in the
latter. The 1971 level was close to 2.0 million tons, and the estimate for
1972 is about 3.4 million tons. Soybean yield has, however, remained at close
to 1.2 tons/ha over the 1950's and 1960's, mostly because of the rapid expan-
sion in area cultivated on heretofore undeveloped or pasture lands. Between
1952-54 and 1969-71, area planted rose by 19.3% per year while yield has re-
mained relatively stagnant (Appendix 3-1). Projecting soybean production is
extremely difficult because of its volatile growth in recent years and because
it is a relatively new crop for Brazil. Estimates for 1976 made by the last
IBRD economic mission range between 4.1 million tons and 6.8 million tons,
mostly due to further large increases in area planted, but also due to an
expected modest growth in yield. At the current pace of expansion in area
devoted to soybeans, especially in the states of Goias, NIato Grosso and Minas
Gerais, soybean production in Brazil will continue to exhibit substantial
growth.
2.        Other Oilseeds.  Cottonseed is probably the second most important
oilseed input for project facilities. As a by-product of cotton, its growth
in output is very much dependent on the market for cotton lint. Yearly
cottonseed production rose from an average of 0.79 million tons in 1952-54
to 1.24 million tons in 1969-71, an annual growth rate of 2.7%. With area
planted growing at 2.8% per year, average yield remained at essentially the
same level over the two decades (Appendix 3-1). Future growth in cottonseed
production would depend upon the behavior of the market for cotton lint;
rising competition from man-made fibers should cause the growth rate in
cottonseed production to fall below its level of the past two decades.
3.        Farm production of peanuts grew at an annual compound growth rate
of 10.7% from 1952-54 to 1969-71, from a yearly average of 115,000 tons to
644,000 tons (in shelled peanuts). Area planted rose at an annual rate of
9.5%, while yield increased from 825 kg/ha to 953 kg/ha of shelled nuts,
during the same period. Castor seed production rose from a yearly average



ANNEX 3
Page 2
of 163,000 tons in 1952-54 to 366,000 tons in 1968-70, an annual growth rate
of 5.2%, while area planted rose by 3.5% per year. Yield increased from ahi
annual average of 749 kg/ha in 1952-54 to 966 kg/ha in 1968-70 (Appendix 3-1).
Future growth rates in peanut and castor seed output are expected to maintain
their levels of the 1950's and 1960's.
Oil
4.        Soybean oil, although a relatively new product in Brazil compared
to other vegetable oils, exceeded all others in value of production in 1970
(Appendix 3-2). With the recent expansion in soybean processing capacity,
soybean oil is expected to assume the dominant role in Brazil's vegetable
oil industry, both in volume and value of output. Cottonseed oil and castor
oil both averaged around 160,000 tons in 1968-70, followed by soybean oil at
114,000 tons, peanut oil at 96,000 tons, and coconut oil at 84,000 tons.
Corn oil has played a minor role, with only about 7,500 tons. Between 1956-58
and 1968-70, soybean oil production grew at an annual average rate of 22.5%;
cottonseed oil, at 5.5%; peanut oil, at 6.5%; corn oil, at 8.3%; coconut oil,
at 7.6%; and castor oil, at 8.5%.
5.        Except for corn oil, whose average unit value (expressed in 1971
cruzeiros per ton) increased by 38.4% during the period 1956-58 to 1968-70,
vegetable oil in general have been declining in unit value in the last de-
cade and a half. Average unit values in 1956-58 and 1968-70 and overall
percentage changes in these values during the indicated period were as
follows (1971 Cr$/ton):
Soybean   Cottonseed   Peanut    Corn    Coconut        Castor
1956-58         2,226.7    1,979.5       2,581.2  2,190.9  2,277.1       1,580.9
1968-70          1,870.8    1,604.8      1,992.2  3,031.2  1,676.1       1,547.2
Total change:
1956-58 to
1968-70          -16.0%      -18.9%       -22.8%   +38.4%   -26.4%        -2.0%
B. The Industry
Background
6.        Great increases in soybean production since 1965 and the strong
export market for meal have stimulated the soybean industry to grow rapidly.



ANNEX 3
Page 3
In 1966 and 1967, there was a crisis of apparent over-production of oil,
probably due to (a) a shift to ICI (value added tax) that led wholesalers to
dump their stocks on the market and obliged the industry to raise the price
of oil; and (b) imports of low priced fats, which also led to decreased con-
sumption of vegetable oils. The inability to place oil surpluses on the
international market made it necessary to stop production in some companies
and brought about severe economic harm to others. Because of this situation,
the production of edible vegetable oil, which was 333,000 tons in 1966, dropped
to 300,000 tons in 1968. In the latter year and in 1969, 44,000 tons of some
flower seed oil was imported to cover domestic needs. In 1969, a recovery
came about, the industries increased their rates of utilization and produc-
tivity, and production reached 385,000 tons of edible oil.
Structure
7.        Ownership.  Most of the oilseed processing plants are privately
owned. The small and medium sized factories are typically Brazilian family
enterprises, while some of the large factories are controlled by foreign
firms. The seven largest producers of vegetable oils in Brazil together
account for slightly more than 60% (1971) of the total vegetable oil production.
8.        Size.  Factories utilizing mechanical extraction (about half of the
total number) normally have a capacity not exceeding 50 to 60 tons of oilseed
a eay.  The rest have capacities ranging from 100 to 400 tons/day, with the
exception of three large factories, which can handle over 1,000 tons/day.
Utilization of nominal capacity is high, especially for the large plants.
Normally, they work 24 hours a day and up to 360 days a year.
9.        Location.  Oil processing industries were originally located in the
south (Rio Grande do Sul and Santa Catarina), close to where the raw materials
were produced, but, during the last 10 years, new production areas have de-
veloped, especially in Parana, Sao Paulo, and Minas Gerais. The main factory
in the south is near Porto Alegre (Rio Grande do Sul), while two of the
largest factories are situated in Ponta Grossa (Parana), which is close to
one of the new production centers. The new facilities constructed have been
close to urban centers or railways where transportation of raw material and
finished products are ensured.
Plant Operations
10.       Processing.  Soybeans are transported to the processors by train or
truck. In recent years, bulk handling techniques have been developed to re-
place the time consuming and expensive sack handling method. The medium size
and larger processors normally have their own silos for raw material storage,
which enable them to operate continuously during the entire year. These
silos are either placed close to the production areas or at the plants.
Control of the ingoing raw material is critical and requires the use of
cleaners and driers.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
11.       The oil in the soybean is about 18% of weight, and there are three
principal ways of extracting it: (a) mechanical; (b) mechanical plus solvent
extraction; and (c) continuous solvent extraction. Mechanical extraction
leaves an oil residue in the soybean cake of about 4.5 to 5%, while the more
efficient solvent extraction method brings it down to 0.5 to 1%. The combined
mechanical and solvent extraction units are a discontinuous two-step system.
In the first step, oil is extracted by screw presses and, in the second step,
batchwise with solvent. After the solvent extraction, the cake has to be
broken up manually.
12.       The continuous solvent extraction system needs very little labor
input, but it is more difficult to reguilate and control than the other systems.
To date, few continuous systems have been built in Brazil because the invest-
ment cost is significantly higher than for the discontinuous two-step units.
In addition, the two-step system is flexible enough to process different
types of seeds.
13.       After extraction, the meal is heated to evaporate the solvent and
destroy the enzymes. The solvent, normally hexane, is then recovered, dis-
tilled, and circulated back into the process. The extracted meal, sometimes
pelletized, is normally transported in bulk.
14.       S asonalitZ.  To avoid having the processing units stand idle, the
medium and large companies often buy large stocks during the harvest, which
is normally from the end of March through September.  The peak stock is
generally about four to five months of production.
15.       Labor Use.  The oilseed processing industry has been rather labor
intensive, the small units using about one worker to each ton processed per
day, and the large units, one worker per 4 tons/day. 1/ Modernization by
itself would thus reduce the labor requirement of the sector, but the net
result depends on the degree of expansion of output.
16.       Profitability.  World market prices for soybean meal are exception-
ally high at present--about US$140 to US$150 per ton--while prices for oil
remain rather low. Long-term projections of the Bank's commodities section
indicate decreases in price of soybeans, meal, and oil, but the margin for
the processors is still adequate.
17.       Environment.  The oilseed processing industry seems to have little
problem with waste or air pollution. However, it is necessary to ensure that
any plant developed adheres to current regulations and standards. It is
important that (a) the amount of solvent is kept low in effluents from the
plant, (b) good ventilation is provided in the plants to avoid concentration
of hexane gases, and (c) the extraction equipment is so constructed that
solvent (liquid or gas) cpnnot leak out.
',' The figures are related to the complete process.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
18.       Management and Training.  The management of the units seems to be
quite adequate. Most management personnel have been trained in the factories
and are quite familiar with the complete system. When new equipment is in-
stalled, the supplier normally spends considerable time instructing the
buyer in the efficient operation of the equipment. In addition, the equip-
ment used in the different plants is similar, which facilitates the training
of workers and supervisors.
Investment Needs
19.       There is a substantial interest among many of the companies today
in expanding their capacities. Crushing capacity is already being increased,
especially in the state of Parana, where it will rise by nearly 100% between
1972 and 1973. However, the production of soybeans continues to increase and
there is a need for additional processing capacity. In addition, there is,
nevertheless, a need for modernizing the small factories so that they can
adopt the more efficient solvent extraction systems.
Availability of Funds
20.       The analysis made of the sector in 1966 and 1967, while the crisis
was in full stride (para 6), led the Government to adopt a policy of dis-
couraging the installation of new edible oil factories and the expansion of
installed capacity of existing plants. Then, the large soybean harvest in
Rio Grande do Sul and Parana, starting in 1969, as well as increased prices
for soybean meal abroad, led the Government to relax its restrictions. While
the state development banks have provided some of the long-term financing
needed by the industry, their resources are limited.
Equipment Supply
21.       Brazilian manufacturers of oilseed crushing and extraction equip-
ment have extensive experience in the production of machinery for the in-
dustry, especially for plants with a capacity of 400 tons per day or less.
Continuous solvent extractors for capacities of more than 800 tons per day
are normally imported and sometimes modified in Brazil, while most equipment
for oil refining is manufactured in Brazil on license from abroad. Brazilian
firms in the sector often have technical cooperation agreements with the lead-
ing major firms in the US and Europe, which enables them to closely follow
developments in the field.
C. Marketing
22.       The three oilseeds which would be processed under project would be
soybean, cottonseed, and peanut. Processing any of these oilseeds would
result in two principal products, namely: (a) cake or meal, and (b) vegetable
oil.  Cake and meal are mostly utilized as feed while vegetable oil is directly
used for human consumption. Production figures are given in Appendix 3-3.



ANNEX 3
Page 6
Domestic  Sarket
23.       Meal and Cake.  Apparent domestic consumption in 1969-70 of oil-
cakes and meals averaged annually as follows: soybean, 221,000 tons; cotton-
seed, 292,000 tons; and peanut, 27,000 tons; or a total of 540,000 tons for
all three types of oilseed cakes and meals (Appendices 3-4, 3-5, and 3-6).
24.       Between 1964 and 1970, domestic consumption of soybean meal and
cake grew annually at 14.4%. Cottonseed and peanut meals and cakes, on the
other hand, declined annually by 3.3% and 3.9%, respectively, during the
same period. In relation to domestic production, apparent domestic consumption
in 1970 was 33.5% for soybean meal and cake, 61.1% for cottonseed meal and
cake, and 13.5% for peanut meal and cake. The ratio for soybean meal and
cake has been continuously declining, despite the substantial increase in
consumption levels. This is due to the rapid growth over this period of the
soybean processing industry, which has increased its supplies of meal and cake
to foreign markets. The corresponding ratio for cottonseed meal and cake has
also significantly decreased, although it is still rather high, and this is
partly a reflection of the decline in the domestic consumption level. In the
case of peanut meal and cake, a large decline in the ratio of domestic con-
sumption to production is largely explained by the decline in absolute con-
sumption levels.
25.       Two important points give the domestic market of oilcakes and meals
a bright outlook. First, recent efforts in rationalizing hog production,
especially in replacing the present stock of lard types with meat types and
promoting commercial piggeries, should significantly raise the demand for
feed rations and oilcakes and meals in the near future. The largest feed
producer in Brazil has an ongoing program aimed at raising hog production
and meat yields in backyard operations and is sponsoring the importation of
meat-type breeds from the US.  In addition, poultry farming is experiencing
a surge as more and more Brazilians partially shift from beef to relatively
cheaper poultry meat.
26.       Second, there is hardly any direct feeding of cattle in feedlots
at present, although there is a growing awareness of its advantages. This
awareness should eventually evolve into actual practice and establish a
substantial market for cattle feed, such as oilcakes and meals and unpro-
cessed feed grains.
27.       Oil.  Domestic consumption of soybean oil, cottonseed oil, and
peanut oil together grew from 212,000 tons in 1964 to 414,000 tons in 1970,
or an annual growth rate of 12%. The total growth could be attributed to
an annual population growth of 2.7% and a per capita consumption increase
averaging 8.9% per year. The estimated per capita consumption of the three
oils in 1964 was 2.69 kg and in 1970, 4.48 kg.



ANNEX 3
Page 7
28.       Analysis of each individual vegetable oil shows the following
pattern in domestic consumption and prices between 1964 and 1970:
Soybean  Cottonseed   Peanut
Oil        Oil        Oil       Total

(Sums may not equal parts due to rounding)
29.       Brazil's GNP grew at 8.5% per year between 1966 and 1971 (Table
2.2 of Economic Report, Vol. II). If this growth rate is maintained through
1980, along with a projected population rise of 2.8% per year, the average
increase in per capita GNP would be about 5.5% annually.  USDA estimates the
income elasticity of demand for vegetable oils in South America to be about
2.10 1/ and since Brazil is South America's largest country, it may be assumed
that the elasticity estimates for the continent are good approximations for
Brazil. Based on this assumption and on the foregoing projection of population
and per capita income growths, total consumption of vegetable oil would grow
annually at about 11.9% 2/ if the relative average price of the commodity does
not change. The average annual growth rate of vegetable oil production be-
tween 1956 and 1970, derived by regression method, was about 8.3%. If the
same growth rate may be assumed without the project's contribution to veg-
etable oil output, then there would be a continuing annual deficit on this
commodity represented by the gap between the projected higher annual growth
in consumption (11.9%) over that of production (8.3%).  Thus, if the project
does not cause production to increase by more than about 3.3% 3/, the present
high domestic prices, relative to world market prices, may be maintained.
1/   "World Supply and Demand Prospects for Oilseed and Oilseed Products in
1980", March 1971, p. 64.
2/   (2.1 x 5.5%) x 1.028.
3/   Deficit of about 3.6% divided by 108.3%.
.



ANNEX 3

Export Market
30.       Meal and Cake.  About two-thirds of soybean cake and meal production
currently finds its way into export markets.  The proportion for cottonseed
cake and meal is much lower, at less than 40%. For peanut, the proportion was
as high as 86% in 1968, although it was only 70% in 1970.
31.       Between 1964 and 1970, exports of soybean meal and cake grew from
44,000 tons to 525,000, or an average annual growth of 51%. Cottonseed cake
and meal grew at 31% per year, from 26,000 tons to 162,000 tons, during the
same period. Peanut meal and cake grew at an annual rate of about 35%, from
an estimated 34,000 in 1964 to 201,000 in 1970.
32.       World demand for oilseed cakes and meals especially from soybean
is expected to continue to grow as a result of increases in per capita income
and changes in taste patterns toward a higher consumption of meat. The US
Department of Agriculture projects oilcake demand in North America, Japan,
Western Europe, and Oceania to rise to about 37.9 to 43.9 million tons by
1980, compared with 24.6 million tons in 1963-65, or an annual growth rate
of 2.8 to 3.7%. Oilcake production in these countries is projected at 34.2
million tons in 1980, indicating net import requirements for the group of 3.7
to 9.7 million tons. 1/ Viewed against Brazil's 1970 total exports of soybean,
cottonseed, and peanut meals and cakes of 888,000 tons, these 1980 net import
requirements of the western developed countries provide a substantial market
potential for Brazilidn oilcake exports.
33.       Oilcake and meal prices have been rising during the 1960's and
early 1970's. CIF Europe prices for Canadian soybean meal (45% protein) rose
from US$91/ton in 1960 to US$120/ton in1968, a growth of 3.5% per year
(Appendix 3-7), while average FOB prices for Brazilian soybean meal exports
rose from US$64.17/ton in 1964 to US$83.10/ton in 1968 to US$122/ton in 1972,
with annual growth rates of 4.4% between 1964 and 1968 and 8.4% between 1964
and 1972 (Appendix 3-4).
34.       Growth of cottonseed meal prices in the 1960's and early 1970's
followed these patterns: 44/45% protein, CIF Europe, US$79/ton in 1960 to
US$94/ton inI966, or annual rate of increase of 2.9%; and FOB Brazil, from
US$110.69 in 1964 to US$60.70 in 1970, or an annual rate of decline of 9.5%
(Appendices 3-5 and 3-7). Peanut meal price increments during the same
period were: 50% protein, FOB southeastern US$ mills, US$58.97/ton in 1960
to US$82.89/ton, or an annual growth rate of 3.5%; and FOB Brazil, US$65.41/
ton in 1964 to US$78.03/ton in 1970, or an annual increase of 3.0% (Appendices
3-6 and 3-7).
35.       Prices of the three oilcakes and meals mentioned above depend to a
significant degree on availability of fishmeal, aside from the world supply
conditions for the oilcakes and meals themselves. The high correlation in
I/   USDA, World Supply and Demand Prospects for Oilseeds and Oilseed Products
in 1980, Foreign Agricultural Economic Report No. 31, March 1971, pp. 120,
122, and 124.



ANNEX 3
Page 9
world prices between fishmeal and soybean meal (0.89) and between soybean
meal and cottonseed meal (0.91) indicate the strong influence of the supply
availabilities of one commodity on the price of the other. 1/ Fishmeal, whose
output rose an average of 12% a year between 1960 and 1968, dropped in pro-
duction by about 10% in 1969. As a result, production rose an average of
only 1.2% per year from 1968 to 1970. 2/ Since Peru accounted for almost 40%
of total world production from 1967 to 1970, current curtailments in its
output and export have resulted in large increases in oilcake prices. March
1973 quotations of soybean meal in the US midwest are as high as 250% of the
March 1972 price. X/
36.       Thus prices of cake and meal are at unprecedented levels and are
projected by the Bank's commodities section to decline over the decade. This
is based on two assumptions: first, a recovery of fishmeal production and,
second, rapidly increasing supplies of seed and cake/meal, particularly of
soybean. Very large increases in the production of soybeans are feasible as
land is shifted out of the production of other grains, especially wheat and
corn, and as idle land is brought into production. It is also assumed that
world processing capacity would expand rapidly. If a combination of all
these circumstances were to occur, the supply of cake/meal would increase at
a rate exceeding the growth in demand, and this would produce a downward
pressure on prices.
37.       Oil.  In contrast to the domestic vegetable oil market, the export
market prospects for vegetable oils are not bright.  Projections by FAO and
USDA both point to continuing decline in vegetable oil prices well into the
late 1970's. IBRD commodities specialists forecast soybean oil prices (CIF
Europe) to be about US$241 per ton in 1980, compared with US$290 per ton in
1971. Cottonseed oil is projected at US$275 per ton in 1980, compared with
US$349/ton in 1971. Forecast of peanut oil price in 1980 is US$317/ton,
compared with US$395/ton in 1971.
38.       The projected decline in world market price of the principal edible
vegetable oils can be best understood by reference to Appendix 3-8, which
justaposes world production and consumption of those oils between 1967 and
1972. Except for 1968 and 1969, when apparent shortages in these oils were
recorded, world production has been exceeding world consumption and this
demand gap has been growing since 1970 as shown by the ratio of that gap to
consumption, as follows: 1970 - 0.21%; 1971 - 0.86%; and 1972 - 1.62%.
1/   Correlation coefficients computed by USDA and reported in their "World
Supply and Demand Prospects for Oilseeds and Oilseed Products in 1980",
March 1971, p. 16.
2/   UNDP/FAO Indian Ocean Fishery Commission, International Trade -  Fishmeal,
March 1971, p. 11.
3/   Wall Street Journal.



ANNEX 3
Page 10
39.       Studies made by USDA 1/ show that the value of meal and cake gener-
ally exceeded the value of the corresponding oil from a given quantity of
soybean or cottonseed in 1967. The ratios of value of cake to value of oil
for that year were: 67:33 and cottonseed, 52:48. Although peanut showed
just the reverse situation, at a meal value/oil value ratio of only 29.71,
its importance is quantitatively small.  Since 1967, however, the meal value/
oil value ratio must have further increased as a result of relative increases
in meal and cake prices compared to oil prices during this period. Thus, the
oilseed processing industry can be expected to regard oilcake and meal more
as their principal product and oil as merely a secondary product, thus re-
sponding more actively to cake and meal prices. Thus the supply of vegetable
oil would not necessarily be a direct response to market conditions for that
commodity. As long as cake and meal prices are good and the weighted value
of cake/meal and oil makes it profitable to continue oilseed processing oper-
ations, there would be no constraint to the production of oil as a secondary
product and this is the situation the world might well be faced with in the
1970's.
40.       On the basis of the general prognosis of world markets for vegetable
oils, Brazil cannot really look forward to it as an attractive source of foreign
exchange earnings. ,Nonetheless, the nature of the oilseed processing indus-
try, whereby demand and prices of cakes and meals would more likely dictate
the production of oil, may mean that Brazil's domestic vegetable oil produc-
tion will far exceed the 11.9% annual growth in domestic consumption estimated
above.  Using USDA's estimate of the price elasticity of demand for vegetable
oil in South America of -1.21, and considering that the supply function of
vegetable oil would be perfectly inelastic as to price, on the assumption that
it is essentially a by-product of cake and meal, then any excess of supply over
demand at a given price would cause that price to fall by about 83% of the
percentage of the excess supply over the quantity demanded prior to the price
adjustment. Thus, if supply exceeds demand at prevailing prices by 10%, then
prices would fall by 8.3%. Domestic prices would then continue to fall so
long as annual production exceeds annual consumption until those prices reach
the export (FOB Brazil) price for vegetable oils. At that point, excess supply
would find its way into export markets. But this assumes, of course, that
processing would continue to be profitable because of oilcake and meal prices.
D. Financial Models
General
41.       Model A represents an investment in a new soybean processing plant
with a capacity of 300 tons raw material per day. The unit would utilize a

combined mechanical and solvent extraction system. The amount of oil extracted
fromthe beans would increase due to the more efficient extraction system in
comparison with factories working only with mechanical extractors. It is as-
sumed that the factory would work 350 days per year, with a utilization rate
of about 90%.
42.       Since all production is expected to be exported, the prices assumed
are thus projected by the Bank's Commodities Section, which show a decline
up to year 1980. However, the marging to the processors are adequate and
the financial rate of return of the investment is calculated to be 24%.

E. Investment Demand Under the Prolect
43.       The consultants who prepared the project estimated that the invest-
ment needs of the oilseed processing industry in the center-south would amount
to about Cr$47 million (US$8 million) over the project period and this is the
amount included in the proposed project. The PB's, however, estimated the
requirements at about Cr$295 million (US$49 million). The different results
from the fact that the consultants were concerned with the weakness of the
world market for soybean oil and limited their proposal to the amount of
funds they judged necessary for the rationalization of the industry - the
consolidation of small firms to form medium sized firms operating more effi-
cient plants. The PB's did not take this criterion into consideration.
Nevertheless, since the estimate made by the PB's was based primarily on
expressions of interest by potential clients, it serves to indicate that the
demand for long-term financing by firms in the industry is strong.
44.       The following are project investments estimated by the consultants
and by the participating banks for the project period (Cr$ million);
