BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Milk Processing
A. Production
Raw Milk
1.        Farm production of milk rose from a yearly average of 4.3 million
tons in 1956-68 to 7.0 million tons in 1968-70, representing an annual growth
rate of 4.2% (Appendix 4-1). Growth prospects are not clear because of the
existing poor marketing structure, but inclusion of a milk processing com-
ponent under the project could help improve the rate of the past two decades.
Milk Products
2.        Pasteurized liquid milk and powdered milk are the two most important
processed milk products in Brazil, both in terms of volume and value of produc-
tion.  Cheese follows in importance, then butter and condensed milk.  Average
S          annual growth rates in volume of output between 1956-56 and 1968-70 were:
powdered milk, 10%; pasteurized milk, 9.4%; and cheese, 4.2%. Condensed milk
and butter outputs declined, respectively, by 0.3% and 2.1% annually during
the same period. Pasteurized milk production increased appreciably starting
in 1969; taking account of this surge provides an average annual growth rate
between 1954-56 and 1969-71 of 11.1%.
3.        Average unit values remained almost the same for condensed milk,
increased for powdered milk and cheese, and mildly declined for pasteurized
milk and butter during the 10-year period covering 1954-56 to 1964-66 (1966
is the latest year for which value data are available). Unit values per ton
of production and corresponding 10-year changes are as follows (1971 cruzeiros
per ton):
Condensed    Powdered    Pasteurized
Milk         Milk          Milk         Butter    Cheese
1954-1956           2,552        3,629           459         5,947      3,847
1964-1966           2,574        3,956           446         5,710      4,688
Total Change:
1954-56 to
1964-66            +0.9%         +9.0%          -2.8%        -4.0%     +21.9%
Appendix 4-1 shows details of historical series on quantities and values of
production of principal milk products.



ANNEX 4
Page 2
B.  The Industry
Structure
4.        General.  Brazil's tradition in cheese manufacturing and processing
(cooling) of milk for distribution in the urban areas dates back to 1800.
The techniques arrived with immigrants from Europe and the varieties of
cheese manufactured are still typically Danish, German, and Italian. The
industry developed earliest in  inas Gerais and Sao Paulo and later in the
southern states, but the states of Minas Gerais and Sao Paulo are still the
main producers of milk in Brazil, accounting for about 50% of total production.
5.        An estimated 500,000 farmers produce milk, but average production
is low at 40 liters per day. About 10 to 12 million cows are kept for milk
production, with 30% being milked at any one time.- On an average, each cow
produces about 500 to 800 liters per year, which is far below the 3,000 to
4,000 liters per year normal for the US and Europe. About 15% of the national
herd is specialized in milk production, and another 35% is raised for both
milk and beef (Annex 2). Milk production is highly seasonal, because most
dairy cattle are fed mainly on grass; pastures normally dry up during the
winter season, which lasts from June through November.
6.        The general condition of dairies varies widely in Brazil.  The
larger cooperatives and private dairies have high technical standards while
the small ones are often in poor condition, with ineffective cold storage;                 0
old, inefficient equipment; and a low hygienic standard. This situation
results in high losses in the handling of the milk and is often responsible
for the inability of dairies to receive and process excess milk during the
months of high production. The milk then remains at the farms and is most
often fed back to the cattle. In Minas Gerais, it was estimated that about
25% of all milk produced (1968-69) was never processed, partly as a result
of rejections because of poor quality.
7.        Ownership.  Most of the dairies are owned by cooperatives, ranging
from small enterprises, supplying only the local market with pasteurized
milk, to the major dairy cooperatives, supplying the big urban centers with
milk and other products. The large cooperatives are organized on the basis
of regional cooperatives, which are members of a central cooperative. (The
central cooperatives, however, normally have no direct control over the
regional organizations.)
8.        The privately owned dairies are rather large units having a well
developed production program. They produce about 85% of the consumer milk
and about 50% of the powdered milk; the remainder is processed by cooperatives.
The few state-owned dairies provide an insignificant part of Brazil's total
production.
.



ANNEX 4
Page 3
0
9.        Size.  The range in size is substantial.  A study made in 1966 showed
that about 70% of all dairies in Minas Gerais produced from 1,000 liters per
day to 5,000 liters per day.  The same range existed in the state of Sao Paulo.
Since 1966, a number of mergers have taken place, primarily in Sao Paulo, and
some large dairies (800,000 to 1,000,000 liters per day) have been constructed
in the Sao Paulo area. The rate of merger has been slower, however, in Minas
Gerais, which still has about 700 dairies with an average capacity of 9,000
liters per day.
to.       Location.  The milk industries have traditionally been located close
to the production areas because of the problems involved in transporting fresh
milk from the farms to the dairies. The cost of distribution has, for this
reason, been rather high and the areas which have developed most rapidly have
been those close to the large consumer centers. However, with the development
of powdered milk factories and collection centers where the milk is cooled and
transported to the dairies adjacent to the large cities, new supply regions,
such as the northern part of Espirito Santo and the central region of Minas
Gerais, have grown in importance.
Regulations
11.       The milk industry is inspected and regulated by DIPOA, which also
collects statistics on the industry. Implementation of the inspection pro-
gram has been rather slow and covers only about 25 to 30% of the total milk
production.  About 50% of the milk made into powdered milk is inspected by
DIPOA. DIPOA inspects about 950 milk plants, 600 of which are situated in
Minas Gerais. Of the 600 in Minas Gerais, 440 produce consumer milk. The
inspection of plants and products is most efficiently carried out for milk
products which are exported or sold interstate.
lant Operations
12.       Labor Use.  The total milk processing industry, including dairies,
dairy-related industries, and the milk product distribution system, is es-
tinated to employ about 40,000 to 60,000 workers. The work is labor intensive
and mechanization has not been rapid due to low labor costs and the large
number of small dairies. Much of the work which is normally mechanized in
the US and Europe is done by hand in Brazil.
13.       Environment.  The waste water from the dairies is normally disposed
of without any treatment.  Part of the whey, especially from the larger dairies,
is also often discharged, thus raising the degree of pollution of the waste
water over the level prescribed by existing regulations, which are adequate.
DIPOA would approve the general design of new installations under the project,
including the design of appropriate disposal facilities.
.



ANNEX 4
Page 4
14.       Profitability.  The Government of Brazil regulates the price of
milk, both to the producers and to the consumers. The margin to the dairies
is based on a standard calculation of their average production costs but it
does not allow the dairies to earn any significant profit on milk pasteuriza-
tion and packing. Therefore, nearly all dairies produce butter, cheese, and
other products for which the prices are not regulated and the profit margin
is larger. Average profit margins are thus sufficient to provide adequate
investment incentives.
15.       Management and Training.  The large cooperatives and private com-
pvaies have efficient and progressive management, while the medium sized, and
especially the small dairies, appear to be rather weak in these areas. Brazil
does not have any advanced education in milk technology or processing in spite
of the long tradition of its milk industry.
Investment Demand
16.       The dairy sector has, during recent years, put more emphasis on
diversified production. The investments planned by processors during the
next three years would therefore primarily be used for diversification as
well as for some facilities oriented primarily toward powdered milk. The
production of powdered milk and, to a lesser extent, cheese increases the
utilization of excess milk during the wet season and helps to even out the
supply of milk products to the market.  Diversification would raise the profit-
ability of processors and permit increased prices to producers.
Availability of Funds
17.       The state development banks have played an active role in providing
long-term financing to the milk processing industry in the last few years.
Some credit is also available from the National Cooperative Credit Bank, but
it is mostly short-term, for working capital. Short-term financing of work-
ing capital is also available from the commercial banks. The remaining
requirements, which are substantial, are either met from internally generated
funds or needed investments are not undertaken. Additional funds, partic-
ularly for long-term needs, would be utilized readily by the industry.
Equipment Supply
18.       The major European and US equipment suppliers for dairies are re-
presented in Brazil, either through agencies or subsidiaries, and have contri-
buted to the development of a competitive and rather complete supply system.
About 80% of the equipment for regular milk processing lines and up to 50
to 60% of that needed for cheese and yoghurt processing plants are manu-
factured in Brazil. Stainless steel for the equipment is imported. Brazil
seems to have an adequate number of skilled consultants working in the dairy
field and, in addition, some processing knowledge is provided through the
equipment suppliers. For more advanced processing systems, the manufacturers
normally deliver complete lines, including the training of technicians.



ANNEX 4
Page 5
C. Marketing
19.       Mllk products represent an important import of Brazil, totalling
about US$14 million for powdered milk, butter, and cheese in 1970. Brazil
is not expected to become an exporter of milk products in the foreseeable
future.
20.       About 23% of total domestic consumption of powdered milk between
1964 and 1970 was imported. During this period, there was a gradual and
limited decline in this percentage, from 26.7% in 1964 to 22.1% in 1970.
Quantity imported has not followed any consistent trend, mostly because the
product has served essentially as a substitute for pasteurized fresh milk,
but FAO projections indicate an increase in the import requirement to 660,000
tons by 1976. 1/ Imports of butter during the same period averaged around 7%
of total domestic consumption, although annual ratios vary greatly and fail
to reveal any discernible trend. Volume of imports also fails to show any
meaningful pattern. Cheese imports comprised about 0.5% of total domestic
consumption from 1964 to 1970, registering a marked upward trend over this
period, while quantity imported rose ten-fold, from about 60 tons in 1964
to 580 tons, or an annual growth rate of about 46% (Appendix 4-2).
21.       Domestic consumption,of pasteurized fresh milk (which is neither
imported nor exported) has grown from about 209,000 tons in 1954-55 to about
1.1 million tons in 1970-71, or an annual growth rate of 10.7% (Appendix 4-1).
Compared with a population growth rate of less than 3% during the period,
this means a substantial increase in per capita consumption of pasteurized
fresh milk. Consumption of condensed milk, another product which is not
imported or exported, did not vary much (Appendix 4-1).
22.       Domestic consumption of powdered milk, which seems to be an
inferior substitute for pasteurized fresh milk, exhibits the following pat-
tern: whenever production (hence, consumption) of pasteurized fresh milk
was low, domestic consumption of powdered milk would register an appreciable
increase, the opposite being true whenever pasteurized fresh milk output was
high. Domestic consumption rose from 74,000 tons in 1964 to 99,000 tons in
1970 (Appendix 4-2). Mostly because of a decline in domestic production,
total apparent domestic consumption of butter had been falling from 33,300
tons in 1964 to 18,000 tons in 1970, an annual rate of decrease of about 9.3%.
Although no data are available, this fall in butter consumption may be part-
ially explained by increases in consumption of substitutes such as margarine
and cheese and processed cheese spread. Large increases in apparent domestic
consumption of soybean oil from 1964 to 1970 tend to support the argument as
does the rapid rise in cheese imports, coupled with about 4% annual growth-
in per capita cheese consumption. Domestic cheese consumption rose from about
41,000 tons in 1964 to 62,000 tons in 1970, an annual growth of about 7.1%.
1/   FAO, Projections of Agricultural Production, 1970-1980, Vol. I, 1971.



ANNEX 4
Page 6
23.       Average wholesale prices of milk and milk products are shown in
Appendix 4-3. As indicated, the real price of unprocessed fresh milk hardly
changed between 1956 and 1970. This is also essentially true with condensed
milk and pasteurized fresh milk, both of which registered no imports or e>:-
ports during the period covered. Powdered milk and cheese, on the other hand,
showed marked increases, although declines occurred in the later years of
the series. CIF prices, expressed in current US dollars, of imported powdered
milk, butter, and cheese between 1964 and 1970 are presented in Appendix 4-2.
The price of powdered milk imports rose from US$260.5/ton in 1964 to US$614.0/
ton in 1970, an annual increase of about 16%. Butter price, on the other
hand, has a more erratic pattern over this period; however, between the two
terminal years, average price dropped from US$834/ton to US$750/ton, a decline
of 1.8% per year. Cheese CIF price was also erratic during the same period,
registering an overall increase between the two terminal years of 0.9%, from
US$803.6/ton in 1964 to US$847.5/ton in 1970 (Appendix 4-2).
24.       Due to lack of data on average prices of domestically produced
milk products, it is not possible to draw very meaningful comparisons of
absolute prices in recent years. The 22% import component of powdered milk,
valued at US$13.5 million, plus US$537,000 worth of cheese and butter imports,
in 1970 point to significant opportunities for import substitution in the
milk processing industry. Import substitution in powdered milk, butter, and
cheese would not only contribute towards substantial foreign exchange savings
but it is also economically feasible.  As Appendix 4-4 shows, powdered milk
and cheese could hardly be said to enjoy any effective protection from imports; W
on the contrary, for many years the average prices of domestically produced
powdered milk and cheese stayed lower than the average CIF prices of corres-
ponding imports. Butter enjoys rather substantial protection, however.
25.       Projections of the domestic demand for the three milk products
currently imported are shown in Appendix 4-5. Comparison of these projections
for 1976 with consumption levels for 1970 indicate the following growths (in
percent):
Powdered             /1
Milk        Butter-        Cheese
1970 to 1976 - overall                 55.8         197.2         101.7
Average per year                   7.7          14.6          12.4
1970 to 1978 - overall                 79.0         241.5         131.7
Average per year                   7.6          13.1          11.1
1970 to 1980 - overall                105.7         292.4         166.2
Average per year                   7.5          12.1          10.3
/1   The base year figure is not for 1970 but the average for 1966-70.
These growth projections suggest additional opportunities for
import substitution in these products.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
D. Financial Models
26.       General.  The powdered milk plant, Model A, can be regarded as a
typical milk processing plant situated in an area with high production of
milk but distant from any major consumer center. The capacity of the plant
is about 70,000 liters of milk per day, but, due to seasonal fluctuations in
the supply of milk, the plant is assumed to utilize 70% of spray drying cap-
acity during June-September. Besides the production of skim milk powder,
butter is manufactured from the milk fat separated from the milk.
27.       The diversified dairy, Model B, is a typical dairy providing an
urban center with milk products. The product mix is standard. The average
daily intake of 30,000 liters of milk is assumed to vary between 25,000
liters during the dry season to 3;3,000 liters in the wet season. Included
in the investment cost is the cost of training technicians abroad in the
processing of cheese and other milk products.
28.       All of the output would be sold on the domestic market.  Since the
prices of Type C 1/ liquid milk are regulated from the producer to the con-
sumer and leave only a small margin to the processor, the viability of the
enterprises depens on the higher margins obtained on other products. In
order to compare the efficiency and financial viability of the powdered milk
plant with operations outside Brazil, the international price for skim milk
powder has been used instead of the domestic price, which is higher. All
prices are assumed to remain constant.
1/   Milk is graded according to bacterial content.  Type C is the normal
grade sold in Brazil, Type B is produced by a few hygienic dairies,
and Type A is almwst non-existent.
0

ANNEX 5
Page 1
A.  Production
w   1.    Since citrus fruit juice processing has good prospects for future
of production and exports, it has been chosen as an example of the type of
operation which would be included in the miscellaneous category.
2.        Oranges constitute the most important citrus fruit expected to
serve as inputs in the citrus fruit juice processing facilities. Annual
production grew from an average 6.2 billion fruits in 1952-54 to 15.6 billion
fruits in 1969-71, an average yearly growth rate of 5.5%. Area planted rose
by 5.8% annually, thus explaining a slight decrease in yield. Lemon produc-
tion rose by 7.6% per year, from 0.41 billion fruits to 1.32 billion fruits,
between 1952-54 and 1968-70. Area planted grew at a somewhat slower pace
during this period, at 6.0% per year, accounting for the increase in annual
yield from 94,000 fruits/ha to 118,000 fruits/ha. Tangerine farm output
increased at an annual rate of 4.8%, from 1.08 billion fruits to 2.30 billion
fruits, between 1952-54 and 1968-70. Area planted rose by 5.6% per year
during this period, accounting for the decline in average yield (Appendix
5-1). The growth prospects of citrus fruit production appear good as domestic
demand grows stronger with rising income, and juice concentrate exports
experience rapid growth.
B. The Industry
Structure
3.        General.  The first modern export-oriented citrus processing plant
was constructed in Brazil in 1963, closely following the 1962 freeze in
Florida, which pushed prices up to an attractive level. Since then, the
production and enxport of concentrated orange juice has grown rapidly. Sao
Paulo is the main producer of citrus fruit and has the highest concentration
of industries based on this fruit, while Minas Gerais is second. New areas
have been planted in Rio Grande do Sul and Espirito Santo which will come
into production over the period 1973-75. Practically all the large farms
producing citrus are diversified and devote land to other crops, while many
of the farms with less than 20 ha specialize in fruit growing.
4.        Ownership and Size.  Citrus fruit juice processing is at present
dominated by a relatively few large, vertically integrated companies that are
well adapted to exploiting the export market. However, the rapid growth of
the domestic market in the next few years and would allow and medium sized
companies to comnpete effectively. In order for a standard plant to be operated
at a profit, the size of the unit has to be comparatively large, due to high
initial cost. Minimum capacity seems to be around 20 to 25 tons of raw material
per hour.



ANNEX 5
Page 2
0
Plant Operations
5.        Processing.  The processing plants are normally situated close to
the production areas and the handpicked oranges are transported by truck,
often in bulk. The normal storage capacity at the factory is adequate to
hold raw material for about three to four days of processing; there is a
great risk that the oranges will spoil if they are held longer. Before the
oranges are processed, they are washed and graded. The washing operation
must be done carefully to clean away the pesticides sprayed on the fruits or
they will contaminate the juice.
6.        Juice is then extracted from the oranges for a normal yield of 40
to 45%. In the same operation, the peel is squeezed and washed in order to
recover the essential oils. The untreated orange juice is screened and
centrifuged to a standardized pulp content before it is concentrated. The
concentration, which is the essential part of the operation, has to be done
with highly efficient equipment in order to avoid degrading the juice. The
concentrate is then deep frozen. The Brazilian firms also make "cutback",
whereby a part of the fresh juice is added to the concentrate to get a better
taste and aroma.
7,        The concentrate is normally sold in skel drums containing about
250 kg. The essential oil is also exported in drums or glass containers,
while the other by-products, such as peel and seeds, are often sold without
further handling to the local market as cattlefeed.
8.        Production Costs.  The cost of establishing new orange plantations
and the cost of production are comparatively low due to low labor wages. A
recent study made in Espirito Santo shows the total cost, up to the fifth
year, of planting a hectare of orange trees (about 215 trees) to be about
Cr$5,000.
9.        In a normal year, the cost of production runs between Cr$3 to Cr$5
per box (one box - 40.8 kg), and during 1972 the processors paid, on average,
about Cr$6 per box for the oranges delivered to the factories, which, when
calculated on a per ton basis, would be US$25. The price per ton in the US
is about US$73 and in Israel, US$20 to US$25. The Israel orange growers,
however, receive subsidies from the Government.
10.       Seasonality.  The orange harvest for processing in most areas lasts
about eight to 10 months, starting in early May and running until the follow-
ing December-January. This is a rather long harvest period, compared to,
for example, Israel, where it occurs between late December and April. Since
most orange plantations are not irrigated, production is dependent on rainfall,
and the Sao Paulo citrus zones frequently suffer from dry conditions. To
improve the yield and avoid yearly fluctuations, more irrigation systems are
being installed.
.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
11.       Labor Use.  The production of oranges is, with its spraying, picking,
and fertilizing, quite labor intensive. The processing plants also use large
numbers of workers since they operate on a 24-hour basis and need people in
the receiving and grading sections.
12.       Environment.  The pollution from a citrus juice factory is low,
since the by-products are used for feed and there is little effluent to be
discarded.
13.       Profitability.  The profitability of orange juice processing plants
is very dependent on the production costs of the oranges and the price to the
processors. The domestic market for fresh fruit is strong so the processors
buy their raw material in a competitive market. Due to a rather stable world
market price, and a good market for the Brazilian concentrate, processors
have done well in recent years. The long-term price projections for fruit
juice concentrate show an increasing trend, but this would probably affect
the price to the producers more than the profit of the processing companies.
14.       Management and Training.  Processing'operations in Brazil are highly
automated and production is somewhat standardized. Due to the advanced
technical machinery used, it is necessary to have adequate mechanical and
electrical engineers to supervise the operations. Management personnel seem
to be of high standard, and workers employed by the industry normally receive
training at t'he factory. Since nearly every factory performs its own quality
control, the analysts must be well trained.
Investment Needs
15.       The production of oranges and orange juice concentrate has increased
steadily, and it seems probable that Brazil can continue to expand its share
of the total world market. Investments are needed in processing facilities to
handle the output from the new areas planted.
Availability of Funds
16.       The state development banks have provided most of the long-term
loan financing for the industry, but their funds are limited and more financial
support is needed.
Equipment Supply
17.       Processors have generally purchased both new and replacement equip-
ment from foreign manufacturers, partly as a result of the rapid expansion of
the processing industry and the lower risk in purchasing well-known equipment.
However, Brazilian firms manufacture many components at comparable or lower
cost, and processors may be expected to fill more of their needs with local
equipment as it gains acceptance in the industry.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
C. Marketing
18.       Exports of fruit and vegetable juices have grown dynamically, from
minimal quantities in 1964 to more than an estimated 55,000 tons in 1972, of
which 85% is concentrated orange juice. In 1972, all fruit juices accounted
for 23% by volume but 43% by value, of all fruit exports, compared to
negligible quantities in 1964. Ia 1972, concentrated orange juice accounted
for 87% by volume and 90% by value of all fruit juice exports. The growth
has been in response to strong world demand, particularly from Europe, where
germany (FR) is the most important market and in 1970 took 56.9% of Brazil's
35,400 tons of concentrated fruit juice exports. Canada, the US, Netherlands,
Israel, and Sweden are other large markets.
19.       Brazil's exports of concentrated orange juice rose from about
3,800 tons in 1964 to 33,500 tons in 1970, an annual growth rate of 44%. The
average FOB price of these exports rose from US$376/ton in 1964 to US$455/ton
in 1969-70 (Appendix 5-2). Appendix 5-3 shows the amounts and distribution,
by destination, of Brazil's orange juice exports in 1969 and 1970. With
increasing per capita incomes in these countries, it is expected that their
demand for Brazilian orange juice will continue to increase and assure Brazil
of good export markets for this product into the next decade. Nevertheless,
the international processed fruits market will become more competitive in the
future as world market supplies increase. Brazil must therefore continue to
process up export products, retain and improve the present good quality fruit
and juice, and continue to improve per tree yields.
20.       The domestic market for citrus fruit juice is growing rapidly and
by 1975 may exceed the export market as a result of changing taste and income
patterns among the upper income groups in Brazil.
21.       Cold storage facilities for the export of frozen concentrated
orange juice are only just adequate at the moment, but the situation will
improve with completion of a facility at the port of Santos with a static
capacity of 15,000 tons. Transport networks in the states of Sao Paulo
and Espirito Santo, from the orange producing areas to the ports of Santos
and Vitoria, respectively, are adequate.
D. Financial Model
22.       The model consists of concentrated orange juice processing plant
which would produce exclusively for export: two-thirds of output would be
exported as frozen concentrate; the remaining one-third would be sold as
"hot pack" in cans.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
23.       The capacity of the unit would be about average for Brazilian firms
and would produce a standard product mix. 1/ By utilizing other fruits such
as grapefruit and tangerines, it would be possible to increase the utiliza-
tion of the plant over the year. The only additional cost would be the lease
of different-sized cups for the extraction units.
24.       The unit would be equipped with an aroma recovery system.  The
aroma normally would be sold together with the concentrate, but could be
traded separately. The plant could also produce so-called "cut back", a
concentrate to which fresh juice has been added to improve the flavor. The
production cost and sales price of "cut back" are both slightly lower.
25.       No value has been assumed for the by-products, such as peels and
grits (the central part of the orange). The grits can be used for producing
orange base for soft drinks, but the cost involved in the processing and
transportation of the peels, normally used for livestock feed, often offsets
the additional income.
26.       The price of orange juice concentrate has increased during past
years. However, as a result of increasing production, the price is projected
to fall about 5% between 1972 and 1980.
0
1/   Even if new technologies, such as reverse osmosis and freeze concentra-
tion, have attracted a great deal of interest during recent years, it
is not recommended that these types of units be considered in this opera-
tion, due to high product losses and uncertain operating costs.

ANNEX 6

BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Existing Institutional and Credit Framework
Monetary Policies and Investment Credit
A. Introduction
General
1.        The National Monetary Council formulates national monetary policy,
and the Central Bank (BC), as the executive agency, is responsible for the
control and inspection of all public and private banks. BC is also responsible
for keeping credit expansion in line with financial policy and for directing
credit to priority areas. The Bank of Brazil (BB), by far the largest com-
mercial bank in the country, is an important instrument of policy and source
of credit, as are the National Bank for Economic Development (BNDE) and the
regional and state development banks. Other important elements in the finan-
cial system are the state and private commercial banks, the Federal Savings
Bank and State Savings Bank, cooperative lending associations, and loan fi-
nance and investment companies.
2.        Funds are available for industrial investment from BC through its
General Fund for Agriculture and Industry (FUNAGRI); BB, although on a limit-
ed scale (loans outstanding for industrial investment at end 1971 totaled
only Cr$554 million - 2.4% of total loan balance); BNDE; and the regional and
state development banks. BNDE's direct industrial investment lending is mostly
in the form of large loans--upwards of Cr$2 million--as it does not have suf-
ficient branch coverage to service a number of small loans. In this category
are included loans from the Fund for Economic Modernization (FRE), which
provides finance for large-scale projects considered vital to the national
economy, and from the Fund for Industrial Modernization and Reorganization
(FMRI), the primary objective of which is to assist in the modernization of
Brazilian industry by financing company mergers and reorganization. The
special Agency for Industrial Financing (FINAME) promotes the manufacture
and marketing of national capital goods. Its loans are channeled through
regional and state development banks as are loans from the Financing Program
for Small and Medium Size Companies (FIPEME), which assists the expansion
and diversification of small and medium sized enterprises. Loans from FIPEME
bear interest at 4% on outstanding balances, with monetary correction cal-
culated according to the ORTN index, and totaled Cr$401 million in 1971,
an increase of 63% in nominal terms over the previous year. In addition to
acting as agents for BC and BNDE funds, most of the regional and state
development banks provide investment credit for industry from their own
resources and from state-financed funds.



ANNEX 6
Page 2
Development Banks
3.        The state development banks that would participate in the project-
those of Minas Gerais, Espirito Santo, Sao Paulo, and Parana-are comparative-
ly new institutions, all of them having been established within the last
decade, some as successors to state development corporations. They are wholly
or nearly wholly owned enterprises of the respective state governments. An-
other participating bank would be the Regional Development Bank of the Extreme
South, which is owned jointly, in equal shares, by the governments of the
states of Rio Grande do Sul, Parana, and Santa Catarina. Each bank is gov-
erned by specific laws enacted by the state legislatures but all are subject
of the overall control of BC under the terms of Central Bank Resolution No.
93 of 1968, which regulates the constitution and functions of regional and
state development banks. Resolution No. 93 provides for BC inspection of
development bank accounts and its prior approval of all appointments to Boards
of Directors. It does not permit state banks to operate branch offices but
this limitation does not apply to the regional banks. In any case, it is not
considered to be a constraint on activities. Development banks are permitted
to participate in the equity of other companies up to a limit of 30% of their
capital and free reserves, with the provision that such participation shall
be at the time of formation of the company or when there is an increase in
the company's share capital. The banks are not permitted to trade in equities
and equity investments in any one company and are expected by BC to be for
only a limited period.  The development banks are permitted to borrow up to
an amount equal to 10 times their capital and free reserves, but guarantees
given by the bank, restricted to the equivalent of five times the capital and
free reserves, reduces the borrowing limit by the amount of the guarantees.
The BC regulation limits the term of development bank loans to 12 years.
4.        Resources.  In addition to their own resources, which comprise
capital, accumulated reserves, and, in the case of a few banks, deposits
from the public, development banks have access to various federal and state
funds. The principal sources of Federal funds are BC and the National Devel-
opment Bank (BNDE), although the Fund for Studies and Project Planning (FINEP),
controlled by the Federal Ministry of Planning, is being increasingly used to
finance project feasibility studies. The General Fund for Agriculture and
Industry (FUNAGRI), administered by BC, and the Fund for the Development of
Small and Medium Industry (FIPEME) and the Special Industrial Financing Agency
(FINAME), administered by BNDE, are of particular importance. The ratio
of external funds to own resources has, until recently, been of the order
of 3:1 but this ratio is tending to increase as more external funds beco"e
available, particularly from BNDE.
5.        Policies.  The development banks are important financial agencies
for the implementation of state development policies and constitute a major
source of long-term finance within the respective states. Their operational
activities are greatly influenced by national and, particularly, state
development policies and they are increasingly seeking to identify sectors
and sub-sectors in which investments would have the optimum effect on
development strategy.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
6.        Lending Terms.  Lending rates vary substantially, depending on loan
purposes and source of funds. Most loans are subject to some form of monetary
correction, which may be at a rate fixed at the outset of the loan or fluc-
tuating according to the movements of either the Treasury Bond Index (ORTN),
which is linked to the internal price index, or to the cruzeiro/dollar exchange
rate. Loans from FINEP funds carry an interest rate of 6% per annum to sub-
borrowers, with a monetary correction of 10% on the outstanding balance.
FIPEME-financed loans have an interest rate of 4% per annum on outstanding
balances adjusted for the change in the ORTN index, while long-term loans from
FINAME funds bear interest at 7% per annum on outstanding balances subjected
to ORTN correction. The Banks' margins on externally financed loans are
between 2% and 3% and charges for funds from banks' own resources, which
normally constitute 20% to 30% of the amount of each loan, are generally in
line with the charges on the external funds. Principal and interest payments
are generally made half-yearly and any adjustment is payable on the dates on
which interest falls due.
7.        Reserves for Bad Debts.  The debt collection record of the develop-
ment banks to date has been good.  However, loan portfolios have expanded
rapidly during the past two years, and, since much of the recent lending is
still within the grace period, the present incidence of default in relation
to loan balances outstanding (generally about 1%) may not be indicative of the
true situation. The banks have hitherto relied heavily on loan security cover
(mortgage on real estate or similar assets to a value of up to 160% of the
loan granted) to protect themselves against default and, except for the State
Development Bank of Minas Gerais (para 16), have not established reserves
for bad debts.
8.        Appraisal Procedures.  Participating Development Banks (PDBs) have
established application and appraisal procedures for industrial (including
agro-industrial) loan applications, which are, basically, quite sound. Ap-
plicants are required to complete a questionnaire detailing their existing
operations and proposed investment and if it appears that further investiga-
tion is needed, a feasibility study of the proposed investment is required.
The PDB issues guidelines for the study and, in some cases, provides tech-
nical assistances. Following submission of the feasibility study, the PDB
assigns a team of technical personnel, typically an economist, an engineer,
and a lawyer, and occasionally, a financial analyst, to carry out a detailed
assessment of the applicant and an appraisal of the projected investment.
9.        Weaknesses in the procedure are that feasibility studies are some-
times inadequately prepared, owing to the applicant's lack of expertise and
unwillingness to hire consultant assistance, or, alternatively, that bank
personnel, who are limited in numbers, prepare studies that will later be
appraised by colleagues. Appraisals, while generally well done, pay too little
regard to managerial competence, and financial rates of return are not calculated.
Furthermore, appraisal judgments are influenced by the quality of loan security
cover.



ANNEX 6
Page 4
0
10.       Accounts and Auditing.  PDB's accounting methods are satisfactory
and their accounts are subject to inspection by Inspectoria do Sistema Bancaria
(ISBAN), a department of BC. ISBAN inspections, which are spontaneous and made
not less than once a year, are rigorously pursued but, owing to time and
personnel constraints, are inevitably limited in scope; furthermore, they
do not constitute an audit in the generally accepted sense, being concerned
primarily with ensuring compliance with banking laws and regulations. Each
of the PDBs has also arranged with a reputable audit firm for an additional
audit of its 1972 accounts, such arrangements being entirely at the discre-
tion of the managements of the development banks, since they are not required
by law.
B. Description of Participating Banks
Banco de Desenvolvimento de Minas Gerais (State Development Bank of Minas Gerais)
11.       Banco de Desenvolvimento de Minas Gerais (BDMG) was established in
1962 in Belo Horizonte and commenced operations the following year. Capital
authorized and fully paid is Cr$100 million (to be increased to Cr$150 mil-
lion in 1972).
12.       BDMG, wholly owned by the state government, works in close cooper-
ation with the Institute of Industrial Development of Minas Gerais (INDI) and
within a policy framework laid down by the Administrative Council of the bank.
The Council is composed of the President of the Bank and four other members,
all of whom are nominated by the stat Governor and serve for a period of five
years.
13.       Executive functions of the bank are under the control of the Board
of Directors, comprising the President and four members nominated by the
President and appointed by the state Governor, subject to the approval of
the Central Bank. The directors serve for five years, but, during the bank's
10-year history, it has had seven Presidents (average tenure less than 18
months) and turnover of directors and senior staff has been heavy. The pre-
sent President and his directors, however, have been on the job for two years
and have instituted a complete reorganization of BCMG's structure and methodo-
logy. Under the new system, the bank has three operational divisions -- indus-
trial, mineral, and rural - each under the control of a director. It will
also become more aggressive in furthering its lending activities, taking the
initiative in suggesting possible development investments to selected enter-
prises. As part of its reorganization, BDMG is also establishing, with the
assistance of the audit firm, Arthur Anderson & Co., a training program that
will lead to the setting up of an internal inspection and audit section and
it will train staff in the financial aspects of loan appraisal and supervision.
14.       BDMG lending at end September 1972 totaled Cr$466.7 million (US$83.3
million), of which Cr$397 million was for industrial projects. Cr$128.5
million of the total was from BDMG's own resources and the remainder from



ANNEX 6
Page 5
0
BNDE (Cr$100 mi:llion), BC (Cr$75 million), National Housing Bank (Cr$129 mil-
lion), and other special funds (Cr$34 million).
15.       Prior to the institution of a special campaign in September 1972,
arrears of interest and principal repayments totaled Cr$29 million. During
the first month of the campaign, however, more than half the arrears were
collected and a further substantial amount was brought in October. It was
anticipated that arrears would be cleared by the end of 1972 except for some
old debts totaling about Cr$3 million, the collection of which had been
delayed by slow court procedures. A reserve fund, established in 1972, of
Cr$1 million partially offsets this. Administration costs for 1970/71 were
3.4% of average loan balances. Appendix 7-1 gives further details.
Banco de Desenvolvimento de Espirito Santo (State Development Bank of
Espirito Santo)
16.       Banco de Desenvolvimento de Espirito Santo (BANDES) was established
in Vitoria in January 1970 as the successor to the Economic Development
Corporation of Espirito Santo (CONDES). Capital, authorized and fully paid,
is Cr$24 million, of which more than 98% was subscribed by the state govern-
ment, including Cr$10.03 million in the form of shareholdings in other entities.
17.       BANDES is governed by a Board of Directors, comprising the President
and three other Directors appointed for three-year terms by the shareholders,
effectively the state government, with the approval of BC.  Organizationally,
BANDES has three divisions:  Programs, Operations, and Administration, sub-
divided into departments responsible for resources mobilization, program
studies, project analysis, contracts, accounts, and administration. Staff at
end November 1972 totaled 113 and included 10 economists, three agricultural
engineers, one mechanical engineer, four civil engineers, three lawyers, and
eight accountants. BANDES reportedly has experienced difficulty in recruit-
ing staff with postgraduate qualifications.
18.       While BANDES has access to federal development funds, it can draw
on them only to a limited extent, owing to the restricted amount of its own
loanable resources (42% of capital is represented by equity script). Agencies
responsible for federal development funds normally require that participating
banks provide counterpart funds of the order of 20% to 30% of loans. The
state goverment proposes to increase BANDES' liquidity by waiving its require-
ment that the 6% annual dividend to shareholders be paid in cash, withdrawing
instead an equivalent amount of the equity holdings forming part of BANDES
capital.  The Government also proposes to increase BANDES' share capital by
appropriation of part of the federal income tax refund which Espirito Santo
receives as an underdeveloped state. Normally one-tenth of the 5% of federal
income tax generated in Espirito Santo returned to the state is allocated to
the Fund for the Development of Agro-Industry (FUNDAI), which is administered
by BANDES on behalf of the state government. The 1973 allocation, however,
will be made directly to BANDES to augment its share capital. BANDES also
acts as agent for the State Development Investment Fund from which the state
.



ANNEX 6
Page 6
government provides finance to selected companies, usually in the form of
equity participation.  The fund is kept as a deposit with BANDES, who uses it
to finance short-term lending operations.
19.       BANDES loans outstanding at end September 1972 totaled Cr$88 mil-
lion (US$15.9 million), an increase of 44% over the corresponding figure at
end December 1971. More than 40% of balances outstanding at end September
1972 were for industrial investment loans. Appendix 6-2 is a condensed
balance sheet for 1969-71.
20.       BANDES is a small but energetically managed bank, acutely conscious
of the importance of its role in the economic development of the state. How-
ever, its manpower resources are fully stretched with its existing operations
and, as consultant expertise is not readily available in the state, it would
need particular assistance from BC in the appraisal and administration of
project sub-loans.
Banco de Desenvolvimento do Estado do Sao Paulo (State Development Bank of
Sao Paulo)
21.       Banco de Desenvolvimento do Estado Sao Paulo (BADESP), which is
headquartered in the city of Sao Paulo, was established by the State Govern-
ment of Sao Paulo in June 1970, under the guidance of the National Develop-
ment Bank (BNDE), with an authorized capital of Cr$140 million (initially
Cr$70 million paid up, but now fully paid). The capital is, at present,
wholly owned by the state government, but BADESP's governing legislation
provides that up to 49% of its capital may be owned by the public.  The bank
is a major financial instrument for the implementation of the state's economic
development policies and as such operates within a broad policy framework
established by the State Financial Coordianting Board.
22.       The Board of Directors, comprising a President and four members, is
nominated by the state government, subject to the approval of BC. Members,
who retire by rotation, serve for a four-year period. The directors, all of
whom have had considerable experience in banking and commerce, are full-time
officials of BADESP and are each responsible for one or more of the depart-
ments of the bank, namely Administration, Resources and Capital Markets, Rural
Operations, Industrial Operations, and Special Operations.
23.       BADESP has a staff of 219 (November 1972), of whom 42 are econom-
ists, 11 are engineers, 10 are business administrators, 17 are lawyers, and
eight are agriculturists. The bank maintains a list of approved consultants,
from among whom expertise is hired to supplement that of its own staff in the
appraisal of projects submitted for financing. The approved consultant list
is made available to loan applicants who must submit feasibility studies.
24.       BADESP's resources at end of June 1972 totaled Cr$180 million
(US$32.1 million), and it had loans outstanding in the amount of Cr$99 mil-
lion (US$17.7 million), of which Cr$48 million (US$8.6 million) was for in-
dustrial investment. Appendix 6-3 presents further details. BADESP requires
that loans be secured by a charge on real estate valued at not less than 130%



ANNEX 6
Page 7
of the amount of the loan. Security valuations are normally carried out by
BADESP staff, but, where specialized knowledge is required, professional
valuers are employed. BADESP does not normally provide loans for working
capital in excess of 20% of the borrower's total working capital requirements.
The bank's operations thus far are of too short duration to allow an estimate
to be made of the quality of its lending in terms of repayment experience,
since most loans are still in the grace period.
Banco de Desenvolvimento do Parana (State Development Bank of Parana)
25.       Banco de Deenvolvimento do Parana (BADEP) was established in 1968
in Curitiba as the successor to Parana Economic Development Corporation
(CONDEPAR), which, since 1962, had been involved in primary agriculture and
infrastructural development in the state of Parana. More than 99% of the
Cr$120 million (US$20 million) share capital is directly owned by the state
government while the balance is owned by several state-controlled organiza-
tions. The President of BADEP and its five directors are appointed by the
shareholders, effectively the state government, with the approval of BC.
26.       Organizationally, BADEP is divided into nine departments--Controllers,
Legal, Accounts, Administration, Analysis, Expansion, Agricultural Operations,
Industrial Operations and Special Operations. The three operations departments
are under the control of the Technical Director, who is a civil engineer,
assisted by approximately 200 staff, 26 of whom are economists, 11 are en-
gineers, 17 are accountants, 11 are lawyers, and five are agriculturists.
27.       As of October 31, 1972, BADEP's cruzeiro loans outstanding 1/ totalled
Cr$323.5 million (US$57.8 million), of which Cr$234.2 million (US$44.8 million)
was for industrial projects. Cr$152.7 million (US$27.3 million), 47% of the
total, was financed from BADEP's own resources, while 42% (Cr$133.4 million)
was financed from federal special funds and 12% (Cr$37.4 million) from the
State Economic Development Fund (FDE). FDE, which is administered by BADEP
for an annual commision of 4% of the value of the fund, is financed from
state budgetaxy allocations and, in addition to providing loan funds, princi-
pally to the industrial sector, is a source of equity capital for selected
enterprises contributing to the economic development of the state.
28.       Until recently, BADEP's lending was largely a result of the initia-
tive of investors, but, with the assistance of two recently established state
organizations, the Economic Promotion Center and the Institute of Economic
Studies, BADEP has carried out surveys which made it possible to initiate
and guide development investment. BADEP states that it has identified demand
1/ On October 31, 1972, BADEP was administering foreign currency loans
totaling Cr$62.4 million (US$10.4 million) for state infrastructural
development.



ANNEX 6
Page 8
for project-type investments totaling Cr$317 million (US$56.6 million) in-
cluding Cr$113 million for oilseed processing, Cr$77 million for meat proces-
sing, Cr$62 million for fruit juice production, and Cr$57 million for dairy
product processing. Appendix 6-4 presents a balance sheet and income and
expenditure accounts for 1969-71.
29.       BADEP has dynamic management and debt collection procedures are
good. Security requirements, which are normally real estate valued at 150%
of loan advanced, are rigidly enforced. Some loans taken over from its pre-
dessor have proved difficult to collect, but it is not expected that they
will result in eventual loss to the bank.
Banco Regional de Desenvolvimento do Extremo Sul (Regional Development Bank
of the Extreme South)
30.       Banco Regional de Desenvolvimento do Extremo Sul (BRDE) commence
operations in 1962 following an agreement reached the previous year between
the Governors of the three southern states of Brazil to establish a devel-
opment bank which would assist the development of the region. BRDE has
agencies in the capital cities of each of the participating states: Porto
Alegre in Rio Grande do Sul, Curitiba in Parana, and Florianopolis in Santa
Catarina. The banks's capital of Cr$33 million is owned in equal shares by
the participating states and BRDE does not pay dividends on that capital.
Simultaneously with the establishment of BRDE, the Council for the Develop-
ment of the Extreme South (CODESUL) was formed to engage in regional develop-
ment planning and to establish a policy framework within which BRDE would
operate.
31.       BRDE is governed by a Joint Administrative Staff which comprises 15
members: the President of the bank, who is also chairman of the Joint
Administrative Staff; three representatives of the Federal Government, each
residing in one of the participating states; two representatives from each
state; the four directors of the bank; and the Executive Secretary of CODESUL.
Responsible to the Joint Administrative Staff for executive control of BRDE
is a Board of Directors, comprising the President, nominated jointly by the
state governors; one director from each participating state, appointed by
the respective state governor with the approval of BC; and one director
nominated by the Federal President on the advice of BC.
32.       BRDE is organizationally divided into departments for rural credit,
industrial credit, and special credit, the latter being responsible for
operations which do not, by definition, come within the sphere of either of
the other departments, such as loans to the public sector. Among the staff,
totaling 309 at end November 1972, are 47 economists, 15 lawyers, 11 account-
ants, 32 civil and mechancial engineers, and 11 agricultural engineers.
33.       As with the state development banks, BRDE's lending resources comprise
its own capital and free reserves and it has access to various federal funds.



ANNEX 6
Page 9
Additionally, however, it receives an annual interest-free contribution from
the three member states in an amount equal to 1% of each of the states' total
annual ICM revenues.  BRDE's lending has increased substantially over recent
years: loan balances, in current cruzeiros, increased by 52% between 1969
and 1970 and by a further 50% between 1970 and 1971, the principal gains
being in loans to the industrial sector. Appendix 6-5 presents a blance
sheet and income and expenditure accounts for 1969-71.
34.       BRDE lends up to 80% of the total cost of expansion or improvement
of an existing investment but loans for new installations are limited to 60%
of cost. For working capital loans, BRDE lends up to twice average monthly
sales, or, in cases where additional working capital is required to increase
production, it will lend up to 60% of the amount of the additional capital
provided by the borrower.
35.       Individual directors may approve loans up to Cr$1.2 million, while
loans up to Cr$3.6 million may be approved by the Board of Directors, which
meets at least once per month. Loans in excess of Cr$3.6 million are subject
to approval by the Joint Administrative Council, which is also scheduled to
meet once per month.
Bank of Brazil
36.       Founded in 1833, BB (Assets almost Cr$48 billion, or US$8.6 billion
at end 1971) performed the functions of a central bank until 1964 and still
acts as financial agent for the Federal Treasury and other government agencies.
Its main objective, however, is to promote and advance national production
(it is the executing agency for the Grain Storage Project--Loan 857-BR).
Government holds 55% of BB's share capital (Cr$1.8 billion, or US$300 million
in 1972) and the rest is held by the private sector.
37.       The President of the Republic nominates BB's president and one
director, while 11 directors are elected by shareholders. The president of
the bank, who is assisted by a technical and legal advisor, has 12 divisions
each headed byt a director. Separate divisions deal with administration, per-
sonnel, and foreign exchange and external trade, and an operational division
(DENOP) is responsible for operational efficiency, which entails issuing
standing instructions for the maintenance of standard operating conditions,
particularly with regard to loan procedures. Loan applications are appraised
in accordance with the relevant standing instruction. Seven regional divisions,
four of which would be involved with the project, deal with banking operations
in their respective areas. The directors of all divisions and their staff are
located at head office. At end September 1972, BB had 810 branches distributed
throughout Brazil, 599 of which were in the 10 states comprising the project
area. BB is subject to inspection by the ISBAN department of BC.
38.       BB's main source of funds is deposits (short term, sight, and
demand) from the public, banks, other financial institutions, the Treasury
(insterest-free), and BC. In 1969-71, financial charges were 0.5% of average
0



ANNEX 6
Page 10
assets while income from all operations was 7.6%, mainly from interest on
production and commercial loans: the 1969-71 average administration cost
was 3.9% of loans outstanding. Amounts written off during 1971 were equiva-
lent to 0.25% of the total balances of loans outstanding at the end of the
year. After making adequate provisions, BB has shown steadily rising profits
over the past five years and has been able to allocate adequate amount to
reserves.
39.       BB's loans outstanding to the private sector at end 1971 totaled
Cr$22.5 billion (US$4.0 billion), of which Cr$8.7 billion wa' for industry,
including Cr$1.7 billion for food production. Interest rates, mounetary
correction rates, other charges, loan and grace periods, and security
requirements vary according to the size and the purpose of the loan and the
source of funds. Eli 1971, the weighted annual average yield (interest and
other charges) on all loans was 14.7%. Appendix 6-6 presents a balance sheet
and income and expenditure accounts for 1967-71.

ANNEX 10

BRAZIL
AGRO-INDUSTRIES CREDIT PROJECT
Economic Justification
1.        For the purpose of evaluating the project's economic feasibility,
an economic rate of return has been calculated for each of the project's
principal components, namely: beef processing, oilseed processing and milk
processing. It would not be practicable to estimate an aggregative economic
rate of return for the project as a whole due to the existence of a mniscel-
laneous component (6.6% of total project investment) which cannot be iden-
tified and whose costs and benefits cannot therefore be quantified. Moreover,
due to the lack of direct inter-dependence among project components, the
individual calculations by component allow a more meaningful assessment of
the project's overall economic feasibility.
2.        The best-estimate economic rates of return of the three principal
project components are:
a)   Beef processing           53%
b)   Oilseed processing        23%
c)   Milk processing           48%
Sensitivity tests of these rates, in relation to certain variations in costs
and benefits, are presented in Appendix 10-1. The price assumptions under-
lying the best-estimate costs and benefits used to arrive at the above rates
of return are presented in Appendix 10-2. This appendix also lists the
corresponding prices used for financial evaluation of the models in Arnexes
2-4.
3.        The basic assumptions on output quantities and applicable prices
which affect the economic rates of return are as follows:
Beef Processing
a)   New plants:
i)   Beef quarters:
a)   Export oriented - total production, valued at Cr$4,905/ton;
b)   Non-export oriented - total production, valued at
Cr$4,690/ton.
ii) Miscellaneous products - total production, valued at the
corresponding prices shown in Appendix 10-2.
b) PlarLt improvement, with accompanying increase in capacity and
a change from domestic to export orientation:



ANNEX 10
Page 2
i)   Beef quarters -- total production without the project,
valued at Cr$ 430/ton; plus all incremental production
attributable to the project, valued at Cr$ 4,905/ton.
ii)   Miscellaneous products -- total production without the
project, valued at 10% of the corresponding prices shown
in Appendix 10-2; plus all incremental production attri-
butable to the project, valued at the full prices shown
in Appendix 10-2.
c)   Plant improvement, without accompanying increase in capacity but
with a change from domestic to export orientation:
i)   Beef quarters -- total production, valued at Cr$ 430/ton.
ii)   Miscellaneous products -- total production, valued at
10% of the corresponding prices shown in Appendix 10-2.
d)   Plant improvement, with accompanying expansion in capacity but
without a change in orientation:
i)   Beef quarters:
1)   Export oriented -- 80% of total production without
the project, valued at Cr$ 430/ton; plus all incre-
mental production attributable to the project,                   0
valued at Cr$ 4,905/ton; and
2)  Non-export oriented -  total production without
the project, valued at Cr$ 215/ton; plus all
incremental production attributable to the project,
valued at Cr$ 4,690/ton.
ii)   Miscellaneous products -- total production without the
project, valued at 10% of the corresponding prices shown
in Appendix 10-2; plus all incremental production attri-
butable to the project, valued at the full prices shown
in Appendix 10-2.
e)   Plant improvement, without increase in capacity or a change in
orientation:
i)   Beef quarters:
1)   Export oriented -- 80% of total production, valued
at Cr$ 430/ton; and
2)  Non-export oriented -  total production, valued at
Cr$ 215/ton.
.

ii)   Miscellaneous products -- total production, valued at
10% of the corresponding prices shown in Appendix 10-2.
Oilseed Processing - Total production and total inputs, valued at prices
shown in Appendix 10-2.
Rilk Processing - Total production, and total inputs valued at the economic
shadow prices shown in Appendix 10-2.
